==PAGE:3==
March 2015 
Summary 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
ii 
 
 
 
Summary 
The purpose of this thesis is to investigate the strength development and properties of fresh and 
hardened concrete with sewage sludge ash. The thesis investigates a sewage sludge ash from 
Lynettefællesskabet I/s which comes directly from incineration. An earlier study has investigated 
the same sewage sludge ash, and its influence on mortar samples. The results for the characteristic 
and strength development from the earlier study will be compared with the results from this thesis. 
This is done in order to verify the properties of the ash and control if the ash has the same effect on 
mortar and concrete samples.  
Other byproducts such as coal fly ash and silica fume has been found very useful in concrete 
mixtures. Both products contributes to optimizing the concrete by having pozzolanic activity, 
improves the workability, increase the compressive strength or makes the concrete more resistance 
to chemicals and alkali silica reactions. If the sewage sludge ash holds some of the same properties 
as coal fly ash or silica fume, it can be beneficial to use this ash in concrete, both from economic 
and environmental reasons. Worldwide the yearly consumption of concrete is 11 billion tons and 
the CO2 emission from producing cement corresponds to 5 % of the global CO2 emission. If the 
sewage sludge ash can be used as a substitute for cement the CO2 emissions could be reduced, and 
a waste product would now be an economical gain for the waste water treatment plant. As it is now 
for the waste water treatment plant Lynetten, is about 50 % use for insolation materials and the rest 
is deposited.  
Tests to determine the characteristic qualities of the sewage sludge ash has been performed in order 
to control if the ash meets Danish requirements and notices for the Danish Ministry of Environment 
such as the content of chloride, heavy metal and phosphorous. It is also tested how the sewage 
sludge ash affects the concrete properties such as setting time, compressive strength, degree of 
hydration, air content, microstructure and chloride migration.  
Results from earlier studies has shown that samples were sewage sludge ash from Lynetten replaces 
10 % cement and 5 % sand in mortar samples obtained the same or a higher compressive strength 
than the reference samples. The studies also indicated that the ash when milled gave a higher 
compressive strength than the untreated. Based on these results were it chosen to investigate 
concrete samples with 10 % cement replaced by sewage sludge ash and 5 % sand replaced by 
sewage sludge ash, called B and F-samples, were the ash used was either milled or untreated.  
The results shows that the amount of chloride in the ash is lower than the limit value and therefore 
not a problem. The content of heavy metals exceeds, for some of them, the requirement and there 
was found 10 % phosphorous in the ash. It could be beneficial to extract some of the heavy metals 
and the phosphorous so that the sewage sludge ash can be used as secondary resource for these 
chemical elements, however, this was not the issue of the present work.  
 
 
==PAGE:4==
March 2015 
Summary 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
iii 
 
 
 
The initial and final setting time increased for the Bample compared to the reference only with few 
minutes. The results for the F-samples showed that the initial and final setting time were the same 
as for the reference. In order to evaluate if the sewage sludge ash meets the physical requirements 
from DS/EN 450-1 for coal fly ash, paste samples with 25 % ash and 75 % cement were 
investigated. The initial setting time must not be more than double for a reference paste with 100 % 
cement. For the test made with paste containing 25 % untreated sewage sludge ash and 75 % 
cement the initial setting time was about three times as long as the reference, and the final setting 
time was not reached within the 860 minute test. The initial setting time for the paste with milled 
ash is 6 minutes longer than the reference. This could indicated that the milled ash have some 
pozzolanic properties, which the untreated ash does not have to the same extent. The reasons why 
the milled ash has a more present pozzolanic activity than the untreated ash, could be that the area 
where the hydration processes takes placed becomes larger when the ash is milled. 
When the compressive strength development for the B-samples and the references are compared 
can it be seen that there is no significant difference in the early strength development. But the 
milled ash seems to influence the late strength development from 28 to 140 days of maturity. The 
B-M sample has the lowest compressive strength at 28 days of maturity but the highest at 140 days. 
When the strength development for the mortar samples and concrete samples is compared is there 
after 7 days of maturity coherence between the compressive strength. But when looking at the 
results after 28 days of maturity, is there not the same tendency as for the 7 day samples. The reason 
is probably linked to the aforementioned late strength development, which the milled ash could 
have when mixed into concrete. The comparison indicate that when the milled ash is mixed into 
mortar is the strength development is more rapid than when the milled ash is mixed into concrete, 
because the concrete with milled ash does not achieve the same compressive strength before after 
about 100 days of maturity. 
When the compressive strength development for the F-samples and the references are compared no 
significant difference in the early strength development is seen. The milled ash seems also to 
influence the late strength development from 42 to 140 days of maturity for the F-samples. The F-M 
sample has the lowest compressive strength at 42 days of maturity but the highest at 140 days.  
Based on the results from the characterization of the sewage sludge ash: chloride migration 
coefficient, setting time, pozzolanic effect and compressive strength tests can it be concluded that 
the sewage sludge ash from Lynetten is suitable for replacing sand or cement in a concrete mix in 
the same amount as for the B and F-samples. Concrete containing Lynetten sewage sludge ash 
could be suitable in constructions which are not depending on the early strength.

Introduction 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
1 
 
 
 
1 Introduction 
From both environmental and economic reasons the usability of ash from waste water plants, 
sewage sludge ash, is interesting to examine. If sludge ash can be used as a substitution for cement 
or sand, in the manufacture of concrete, both the environmental impact from transport and deposit 
of sewage sludge ashes can be eliminated. Furthermore the reduced need for cement, which is a 
heavy environmental cost, means a greener concrete and can save the concrete manufacturer 
material costs. The emission of CO2 from the cement production is 5 % of the global CO2 emission 
(Johannesson, 2012). This means if it is possible to replace 10% cement with the sewage sludge 
ash, is it possible to reduce the global CO2 emissions with 0.5% by using a waste product. In the 
last 50 years the yearly consumption of concrete has increased from 3 billion tons to 11 billion tons 
in the world (Kumar Metha & Monteiro, 2006).  
Earlier waste products as coal fly ash and silica fume, which came from power plants and as a 
byproduct from metal alloying, are now used in concrete in order to improve concrete properties. 
This means that they have changed status from waste product, with a negative environmental 
impact, to a useful product, in concrete production.  
The characteristic qualities of the sewage sludge ash must be determined because, the content of 
salt, the quantity of heavy metals and phosphorous are really important as the salt might cause 
corrosion in a reinforced concrete structure and heavy metals are hazardous and therefore harmful 
to the neighboring environment. Phosphorous is a limited resource and the sewage sludge ash can 
therefore be used as a secondary resource. Earlier studies have showed that sewage sludge ash is 
very different depending on what plant and catchment area the ash comes from. In some cases the 
sewage sludge ash acts as a pozzolane and in addition to the ordinary aggregates in concrete, 
pozzolanes can be added and might have an impact on the strength, the degree of packing and the 
water/cement ratio.  
Earlier studies investigated which influence sewage sludge ash has on mortar samples. This work 
investigates the strength development and properties of fresh and hardened concrete that contains 
sewage sludge ash from Lynettenfællesskabet I/s. The purposes of this thesis is to investigate the 
properties of the sewage sludge ash and how it effects the concretes properties such as setting time, 
compressive strength, degree of hydration, air content, microstructure and chloride migration. The 
results regarding the compressive strength is compared to previous results with the same ash and 
mix design made on mortar samples, in order to verify if there is a coherence between the influence 
on mortar and concrete. 
 
 
==PAGE:9==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
2 
 
 
 
2 Theory 
2.1 Concrete 
Concrete is the most used construction material in the world. 50 years ago the total world 
consumption of concrete was estimated to 3 billion tons, equal to 1 ton for every living human 
being. Today is this number increased to about 1.6 ton for every human being, as the total 
consumption is 11 billion tons (Kumar Mehta, 2006). Concrete has a high compressive strength and 
consists of cement, sand, stones and water. Over the years has it been discovered that adding 
pozzolans such as silica fume and coal fly ash optimizes the concrete. The pozzolans has an 
influence on the chemical compounds that develops the strength of the concrete. Another effect the 
concrete gains from these pozzolans is that the mixture becomes more dense because of the small 
size of the particles, which thereby packs the material better (Jensen, 2008). 
The cement and the pozzolans are both hydraulic binders, and therefore they solidify and harden, 
when water is added. But the pozzolans can only be taking into account as a hydraulic binder, when 
cement is also present. The setting and hardening process for cement can be seen in Figure 1. 
 
Figure 1 - hardening process, from Bygningsingeniørernes materialer - uddrag af materialebogen. 
 
 
==PAGE:10==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
3 
 
 
 
Once this process is initiated, some of the water will be chemically bound to the cement or the 
pozzolans. The mix between these three agents is called the cement paste. The cement paste, with 
the w/c – ratio, possible admixtures and added aggregates determines the workability of the 
concrete.  The slump of a concrete is used to measure the workability. If the slump of a concrete is 
low, the concrete is less workable (Hansen, 2008). If there are added organic substances to the 
cement paste, so called air-entraining agents, is the mixture now called putty mass. These air-
entraining agents are mixed into the concrete in order to improve the frost resistance in the hardened 
concrete. The improvement is possible because the air creates small (5-200μm in diameter) bubbles 
in which the frozen “free” water can expand (Hansen, 2008). In order to make a mixture frost 
resistance, the air content must be at least 4.5 % (Aalborg Portland, 2010). However it is important 
that the air content in a mixture is not too high. If air is mixed into the concrete is the porosity 
increased. This results in a lower strength, if the w/c – ratio remains the same. When sand are added 
to the mixture, is the mixture now called mortar. To get concrete stones needs to be added.  
 
Figure 2 - Mixing stages of concrete, from Bygningsingeniørernes materialer - uddrag af materialebogen. 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
4 
 
 
 
2.1.1 Admixtures 
Admixtures are used to improve the properties of fresh and hardened concrete. They have been used 
as early as by the Romans, who used materials as milk and lard. Admixtures can today be divided 
into chemical and mineral admixtures. The chemical admixtures are mostly used to improve the 
workability of the fresh concrete and as air entraining agents. The mineral admixtures are divided 
into latent hydraulic waste products and non-hydraulic filler materials. The latent hydraulic waste 
products have a pozzolanic effect, when a high pH value is present, and they could be coal fly ash, 
silica fume, slag or rice husk. A pozzolan is a material with cement properties, when combined with 
a calcium hydroxide solution, as the one present in normal cement based concrete. Pozzolans are 
often used to improve the long term strength and durability of the concrete. In many cases is a part 
of the cement replaced by one of the hydraulic waste products to reduce the material cost of the 
concrete and at the same time are the waste product utilized. Another large gain by using one of 
these waste products as a partial replacement for cement is the environmentally benefit, as 5 % of 
the total CO2 emission in the world comes from cement production. A non-hydraulic filler material 
could be finely ground limestone, and is used as a filler in the concrete mixes. This means that the 
material does not have any pozzolanic effect and thereby not react when mixed into concrete. 
Instead it acts as a very fine aggregate/filler which help packing the concrete and thereby increasing 
the strength of the concrete (Johannesson, 2012). 
 
 
==PAGE:12==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
5 
 
 
 
2.1.2 Coal fly ash 
Coal fly ash is an artificial pozzolan, which is a byproduct from coal fired power plants. Coal fly 
ash consists mainly of small spherical particles. When mixing coal fly ash into concrete, it improves 
the workability of the concrete and thereby increasing the slump. This means that the coal fly ash 
reduces the water amount needed. Furthermore coal fly ash tributes to the compressive strength of 
the concrete, which can be seen especially beyond 28 days of maturity. Coal fly ash contains some 
unburned carbon, which can make the use of air entraining agents difficult. This is so because the 
unburned carbon in coal fly ash has a strong tendency to interact with the surfactants in air 
entraining agents. The amount of unburned carbon is often determined by measuring the loss on 
ignition (LOI). As the LOI value of coal fly ash increases, the amount of air entrainment admixture 
required to produce a given air content will increase as well (Hill & Folliard, 2006). 
 
Picture 1 - Coal fly ash, 1500 x zoom. 
==PAGE:13==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
6 
 
 
 
2.1.3 Silica fume 
Silica fume is a pozzolanic byproduct from aluminum production. Normally is the hydration of the 
mineral admixtures slower than the hydration of pure Portland clinker minerals, and the strength 
will therefore be lower at the early age compared to a concrete without mineral admixtures. When 
using silica fume the hydration process is faster than without, and this is partly because silica fume 
consist of very small particles which increases the surface area where the silica fume can react 
(Johannesson, 2012). To obtain a homogenous mix a plasticizer or a super plasticizer is used 
simultaneous to silica fume in concrete, according to DS 481. Some of the positive effects by using 
silica fume in concrete are water tightness, compressive strength and resistant to chemicals and 
alkali silica reactions (ASR) (Aalborg Portland, 2010). The use of silica fume in concrete is so 
popular that the aluminum producers cannot keep up with the concrete producers demand 
(Johannesson, 2012). 
 
Picture 2 - Silica fume, 1500 x zoom. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:14==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
7 
 
 
 
2.1.4 Sewage sludge ash 
Sewage sludge is a waste product from waste water treatment plants. There are three major steps in 
waste water treatment: The mechanical, biological and chemical. The mechanical cleansing 
removes dry matter, as paper etc., with a sieve and afterwards are sand and fats excreted. In the 
biological cleansing are bacteria and microorganism added so that they can degrade organic matter 
and remove nutrients. The last step is the chemical cleansing where a precipitating agent are added 
the waste water. This is one of the processes for extracting the phosphorous. At the waste water 
treatment plant Lynetten an iron based precipitating agent is used, but other metal based agents can 
be used. It is the iron that gives the SSA its reddish colour (Lynettefællesskabet I/S, 2015). 
 
Picture 3 - Sewage sludge ash from Lynetten. 
The sewage sludge at Lynetten is stored in a digester for three weeks where it degrades with 
bacteria and microorganism. The gasses developed in the digester are methane and carbon dioxide 
and are used for heating and electric at the plant. The digested sewage sludge is then dried to a dry 
matter of 40 % to secure a decent combustion. The dried sludge are burned in a fluid bed oven, 
where at the bottom are sand which are kept floating by blowing hot air into the oven. The sand is 
kept in the oven so that it do not lay on the bottom of the oven or leave the oven with the coal fly 
ash. The sludge is pumped into the side of the oven and the temperature of 850 °C secures that the 
heat transfer between sand and sludge happens instantly. The burned sludge ends up as flue gas and 
SSA (Lynettefællesskabet I/S, 2015).  
This cleansing process is for Lynetten and other waste water treatment plants uses other methods 
which means that the end product, the SSA, have very different properties. This means that there 
are as many kinds of sewage sludge ashes as there are treatment plants. A characteristic must be 
made for an ash before used in concrete, in order to determine the properties for the specific ash.  
==PAGE:15==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
8 
 
 
 
2.1.5 Utilization of sewage sludge ash 
Of environmentally reasons it could be of huge interest to utilize SSA. Globally is around 1.7 
million tons of SSA produced.  As it is now is the SSA, from the waste water plant Lynetten, a 
fraction is used for production of insolation material and the rest is deposited (Lynettefællesskabet 
I/S, 2015). Earlier has waste products as silica fume and coal fly ash shown to be useful as concrete 
admixtures and thereby turned into a benefit instead of an expense for the industry. The concrete 
industry benefits by using these waste products in there concrete because each of them has 
properties that improves different types of concrete. It is not desirable to add badges of waste 
products in our concrete just to get rid of them, but finding a purpose so that they replace other 
expensive materials in concrete or as admixtures which improve the concrete. An expensive part of 
the concrete is cement, so it could be of huge interest to replace parts of this by a waste product as 
SSA. In order to replace a part of the cement, the SSA has to have a positive effect on the strength, 
so that the compressive strength of the concrete is not lowered. Earlier studies with Danish sewage 
sludge ashes have indicated that the SSA from Lynetten has a pozzolanic effect, when it is used as a 
partly cement replacement in mortar samples. Studies have also indicated that the SSA could be a 
filler when using it as a sand replacement (Carlsen & Petersen, 2013 and Reiff & Meldgaard, 2013). 
It could be necessary to process the waste product to optimize its properties. The SSA has earlier 
been milled and acid washed to investigate whether it could improve its effect on the concrete 
strength. The studies showed no effect on the strength, when the SSA was acid washed before using 
it as a cement and sand replacement in mortar samples. There were a positive effect on the 
compressive strength for the mortar samples when replacing 10 % of the cement and 5 % of the 
sand with milled SSA (Carlsen & Petersen, 2013 and Reiff & Meldgaard 2013).  
 
 
==PAGE:16==
March 2015 
Theory

2.2 Earlier studies 
In table Table 1 below is a review of earlier works with SSA in concrete. The review is covering 
literature from 1995 to 2012 and the articles are from many parts of the world; Denmark, North 
America, Great Britain, Taiwan, Egypt and Spain. The articles are chosen because they deal with 
the same focus topics of this master thesis; pozzolanic activity, packing, setting time and 
workability.  
Table 1 - List of earlier studies with sewage sludge ash.

Effect of milling and

incinerator sewage

Cement & Concrete 
Composites 32. (2010) S. 
Donatello, A. Freeman-
Pask, M. Tyrer and C. R. 
Cheeseman. 
The paper investigates the 
effect of milling and acid 
washing on the pozzolanic 
activity of incinerator 
sewage sludge ash. The 
pozzolanic activity has been 
evaluated by strength 
activity index (SAI) test and 
with the Frattini test. Coal 
fly ash, metakaolin and 
quartz sand were also tested 
for comparison. 
Milling improved the pozzolanic activity 
of coal fly ash and sewage sludge ash 
according to both the SAI and Frattini 
test. The Frattini test was found to be 
more suitable method for directly 
assessing pozzolanic activity as a range 
of factors can affect the SAI test results. 
Four reasons are mentioned for the 
improved compressive strength; Reduced 
water requirement, larger area where the 
reactions can take place, better packing or 
a combination of these three. 
Comparison of test 
methods to assess 
pozzolanic activity.  
Manuscript (Draft) 2009. 
S. Donatello, M. Tyrer 
and C. R. Cheeseman. 
The paper compares 
different test methods to 
assess the pozzolanic 
activity. Two types of test 
are compared, direct and 
indirect test. The direct tests 
are the Frattini test and the 
Saturated Lime test. The 
indirect test is the Strength 
Activity Index (SAI) test. 
There were correlation between the SAI 
and the Frattini test. But no correlation 
was found with the Saturated Lime test. It 
is recommended to use the SAI and 
Frattini test, because they are well known 
and tested.

pozzolanic activity of 
sewage sludge ash. The raw 
sewage sludge has been 
characterized with different 
methods. It is investigated at 
which temperature the 
sewage sludge should be 
incinerated. 
The sewage sludge contains quartz, 
anhydrite and albite minerals. 
Incineration of sewage sludge at 500-
950°C affects the microstructure and 
pozzolanic activity of the resultant ash. It 
is recommended that the sewage sludge is 
incinerated at 800°C, because 
temperatures lower and higher decreases 
the pozzolanic activity.

==PAGE:19==

12 
 
 
 
As indicated in Table 1 has several different international studies been made in order to investigate 
if a SSA can be turned into a useful product, for example in concrete mixtures, instead of a residue 
to deposit. It has earlier been shown that the use of sewage sludge ashes in concrete mixtures, has 
an impact on the properties of mortar or concrete in many different ways, depending on the ash and 
the way it is used. Several studies indicate that the presence of SSA has an effect on the 
compressive strength. 
In New York in 1995, was it tested and determined that when using the ash as fine aggregate for 
concrete mix, was it possible to replace up to 30 % by weight of the fine aggregates with the ash 
and still reach the design strength (after 28 days). However, the compressive strength decreases as 
the amount of SSA increases. Other studies has shown that by use of SSA as replacement for 
normal coal fly ash, has the compressive strength a tendency to be lowered by increasing the 
amount of SSA. Using SSA in mortar or concrete as replacement for cement, as in Study on cement 
mortar and concrete made with sewage sludge ash, indicates also that the ash has an impact on the 
compressive strength development. The paper concludes that the ash used could have potential 
pozzolanic effect, but the workability and the strength were reduced when the sludge ash was used. 
The limit was up to 10 % of the cement could be replaced by the SSA, and still achieves the design 
compressive strength. An increase in the initial and final setting times has been observed when the 
SSA percentage increases. 
The impact sewages sludge ash has on mortar properties can be different if the ash is mechanical 
grinded. As mentioned in Table 1, increases the workability and compressive strength of mortar 
samples when the fineness is increased (Pan, Tseng, Lee & Lee, 2003). According to the report the 
increases are due to two different things. The workability is increased because of the lubricant effect 
and morphology improvement the grinded SSA provides, and the increase in the compressive 
strength is primarily because of the improvement of pozzolanic activity which apparently increases 
when the fineness of SSA increases. The setting time are, both initial and final, prolonged when the 
fineness increases. According to (Donatello, Freeman, Tyrer & Cheeseman, 2009) could there be 
four reasons that the milled ash increases the compressive strength; reduced water requirement, 
larger area where the reactions can take place, better packing or a combination of these three. 
Different comparisons of test methods to measure the pozzolanic activity have been carried out, 
both directly and indirectly. There were correlation between the Strength Activity Index (SAI) test 
and the Frattini test. But no correlation was found with the Saturated Lime test. It is therefore, in 
Comparison of test methods to assess pozzolanic activity, recommended to use the SAI and Frattini 
test, because they are well known and thoroughly tested.  
 
 
==PAGE:20==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
13 
 
 
 
It has been shown in Evaluation of the Pozzolanic Activity of Sewage Sludge Ash that the sewage 
sludge should be incinerated at 800°C to optimize the pozzolanic activity of the resultant ash. The 
results for the earlier studies indicate that SSA can be used in concrete, but the properties of the 
concrete vary with the properties and the proportion of the ash used. As mentioned in 2.1.4 Sewage 
sludge ash is it important to determine the properties of the ash used before using it in concrete.

March 2015

2.3 Characteristic of the sewage sludge ash 
Before the ash can be used, it is necessary to make a characteristic which determines the risk of 
damage the ash will cause the environment and the properties for the ash. Several laboratory 
experiments are made to investigate this. The two main issues/concerns is the amount of chlorides 
and heavy metals in the ash. 
An ion chromatograph (IC) is used to determine the amount of chlorides in the ash, and the heavy 
metal content is determined by using an Ion Coupled Plasma apparatus (ICP). There are at the 
moment no standard for the use of SSA in concrete in Denmark, but it can be considered as a coal 
fly ash because of the way it is produced. This makes it possible to compare the SSA to Danish 
Standard DS/EN 450-1, which contains requirements for a coal fly ash. The requirements are listed 
in Table 2.

2.3.1 Chlorides 
If the SSA is going to be usable in concrete, is it crucial to determine the content of chloride ions in 
the ash, and afterward the amount of chlorides being leached from concrete cast with SSA. These 
investigations are important in order to consider the risk of corrosion in reinforced concrete and the 
risk of ASR.  
Corrosion of the reinforcement bars will cause a decrease of the strength and durability of a 
construction, because the cross section of the reinforcement bars will be reduced. Additional will 
corrosion cause the concrete to crack, and thereby accelerate the damage. The corrosion process is 
depending on the amount available of oxygen and water. The anode process (Fe  Fe2+ + 2e-) can 
only appears if a cathode process (2H2O + 4e- + O2  4OH-) also is possible. Figure 3 illustrates 
the corrosion process (Hansen, 2008). 
 
Figure 3 - Corrosion process in reinforced concrete. 
Corrosion caused by chlorides is considered as the main durability problem, and it happens often by 
transport of fluid containing chlorides ions and diffusion. The chlorides can be transported in three 
ways in concrete, by diffusion, capillary absorption and by hydrostatic pressure.  
Diffusion is controlled by concentration gradients, which means that there should be a continuous 
liquid phase in the concrete.   The concentration gradient for the chlorides ions occurs when there is 
a small amount of free chloride ions in the concrete and plenty on the outside. With this method can 
the chloride ions be drawn all the way to the reinforcement bars, because there will always be a 
concentration gradient from one point in the concrete to a point closer to the reinforcement.  
Capillary absorption is controlled by moisture gradients. Therefore is this a very common form of 
chloride ion transport in porous construction materials, which are exposed to moistening and drying 
cycles with water that contains chloride ions. This can for example be a splash zone of a bridge. The 
capillary suction forces water that comes across a dry surface into the pore structure. Water from 
the sea and melted snow (if it is from an area that has been de-iced with salt products) contain 
chloride ions, and these can be drawn into the concrete. When the surface in time dries will the 
chloride ions remain inside the concrete. Normally will the transport of chloride ions caused by the 
capillary force, not be transported that far into the concrete, but the core of the structure can still be 
wet and therefore can the chloride ions continue their journey toward the reinforcement bars, by 
diffusion. 
==PAGE:23==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
16 
 
 
 
 
The last transport mechanism is the one caused by hydrostatic pressure, which is controlled by 
pressure gradients. If a concrete structure is exposed to a hydrostatic head on one side and the water 
contains chloride ions, is it possible that the ions will permeate into the concrete.  
 
Because of the great damage chloride ions can cause, Danish Standard provides requirements for 
the amount of chlorides in concrete compared to the amount of cement. The requirements are listed 
in Table 3. 
Table 3 - Requirements for the amount of chlorides compared to cement weight. DS/EN 206-1. 
Application 
Chloride category 
Maximum Cl-content 
compared to cement weight 
Without reinforcement 
CL 1.0 
1.00% 
With reinforcement 
CL 0.20 
0.20% 
CL 0.40 
0.40% 
With pre-stressed 
reinforcement made of steel 
CL 0.10 
0.10% 
CL 0.20 
0.20% 
 
Earlier studies (Carlsen & Petersen, 2013 and Reiff & Meldgaard, 2013) have investigated the 
chloride content for Danish SSA from Lynetten. Mortar samples were cast with different 
proportions of SSA and the amount of chlorides compared to the amount of cement weight was 
calculated. The reports investigated two ashes from the same treatment plant and the results showed 
that all the samples met the requirements from Danish Standard, Table 3.

2.3.2 Environmental classes 
There are 18 different exposure classes in DS/EN 206-1, but these classes are in Denmark discussed 
and therefore the four environmental classes from a former norm system are used: Passive, 
moderate, aggressive and extra aggressive. Passive could be concrete that is exposed in an indoor 
environment where there are no moist, frost or chlorides which could damage the concrete. 
Likewise could an extra aggressive be a bridge pier in a saltwater environment where the concrete is 
exposed to the worst case possible, because moist, chlorides and frost are present. This means that 
the environmental classes decides how strong and thereby how durable the concrete has to be to 
cope with the environment it is exposed to. For reinforced concrete are the compressive strength 
(fck) requirements listed in DS/EN 1992-1-1, and can be seen in Table 4 below. 
Table 4 - Environmental classes and compressive strength. 
Environmental classes Minimum value of the 
compressive strength  
  
[MPa] 
Extra aggresive 
40 
Aggresive 
35 
Moderat 
25 
Passive 
12 
 
The reinforcement is cast into the concrete in order to protect it from the environment. The 
durability of the construction is decided by the concrete quality and by the layer of thickness. A 
source to corrosion could be chlorides, which can come from the outside but also from the 
aggregates or admixtures within the concrete. Therefore there are requirements for the amounts of 
chlorides that are allowed in the concrete. The requirements are listed in Table 3 and are in range 
from 0,1–0,4 % chloride ions relative to the cement weight.

2.3.3 Alkali silica reactions  
The ASRs is dangerous for the concrete because it forms alkali silica gel, and chemical change will 
then appear in the concrete. These changes lead to an expansion in the volume and thereby lead to 
cracks in the structure. The structure will hereafter be the more exposed to moisture penetration, 
which could cause corrosion of the reinforcement bars or frost damage. In order for the ASR to 
occur water, reactive aggregates, high-pH and alkalis must be present. The ASR will not occur if 
one of the four elements is not present. Figure 4 illustrate the conditions for ASR to occur 
(Grottfredsen & Nielsen, 2010).  
 
Figure 4 - conditions for ASR to occur. 
 
 
==PAGE:26==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering

2.3.4 Heavy metals 
In a construction made of concrete containing SSA, is it important to know the amount of heavy 
metals, in order to evaluate and hinder emissions of these toxic elements to the surroundings. As 
well as for the chloride there are different demands for ash depending on the use. These demands 
are, in Denmark, established by the Ministry of Environment. The ministry has established a notice, 
which provides limit values for the content of heavy metals in constructions which are going to be 
placed in environments with risk for leaching. It is called Bekendtgørelsen om anvendelse af affald 
til jordbrugsformål (Slambekendtgørelsen) (Miljøstyrelsen, December 2006). A bio-ash can be an 
ash, which is produced from incineration of sludge, and is therefore useable in this report.  
Another usable notice is the Bekendtgørelsen for restprodukter (Miljøstyrelsen, 2009), which are 
dealing with ashes and slags from incineration. This notice does not have limit values for the 
amount of heavy metals, but categories for the ashes possible uses. Category 1 is for all uses in 
building construction, where category 2 and 3 is for limited use in building construction. The limit 
values from Slambekendtgørelsen and the category index from Bekendtgørelse for restprodukter are 
listed in Table 5 and Table 6.

Another aspect of knowing the content of heavy metals and phosphorous in the ash is to consider 
whether the ash can be a potential secondary resource for especially phosphorous. There is limited 
access to phosphorous from primary sources, and it is therefore important to develop methods, 
which can recover the phosphorous from secondary sources. Earlier studies have indicated that the 
concentration of phosphorous in SSA in general is about 5-10 % per weight (Cyr, Coutand & 
Clasters, 2007). There are different methods to extract the phosphorous from SSA, and the methods 
can be divided in two, thermochemical treatment and chemical extraction (Ottosen, Jensen & 
Kirkelund, 2015).  
Earlier studies (Carlsen & Petersen, 2013 and Reiff & Meldgaard, 2013) have investigated whether 
the SSA from Lynetten met the requirements from Bekendtgørelsen for restprodukter. The results 
showed that the amount of heavy metals in the ash places it in category 2. Experiments regarding 
leaching tests on mortar samples showed that the mortar would be in category 1 when used as a 
waste product. It is not known whether the ash is bound in the mortar samples or that the ash is 
mixed into a larger volume and thereby is diluted.  
2.3.5 Other investigations  
Several other experiments must be made in order to characterize the ash. Experiments such as LOI, 
water content, conductivity, pH and water solubility are important in order to make a full 
characteristic of the SSA.

bachelor project made in 2013, and are listed in Table 7. The two ashes are very different, but 
Lynetten-Ny-1 should be comparable to the achieved results in this master thesis, because they have 
been through the same cleansing process and exposed to the same environment.

2.4 Strength 
The w/c – ratio has a great influence on the compressive strength in concrete. When adding 
pozzolans the w/c – ratio is insufficient but a water/powder (w/p) ratio is used instead, because they 
have similar properties as cement. The different kinds of mixture that are used in this master thesis 
are listed and described in 3.2 Concrete samples. This ratio affects the compressive strength, 
because the water that exceeds the amount of water possible bound in the cement and pozzolans, 
evaporates and leaves small cavities in the concrete. This generates a larger surface area, a higher 
porosity and reduces the strength. In other terms, a high w/c – ratio leads to a lower compressive 
strength for the concrete (Jensen, 2008). 
This becomes clear by looking at Bolomey’s formula, which calculates the compressive strength for 
concrete. 
𝐹𝑐= 𝐾∙( 1
𝑤/𝑐−𝛼) 
Where Fc is compressive strength in the concrete. 
K is a factor, which depends on what type of cement used and the day of maturity.  
w/c is the ratio between the water and the cement. 
α is also a constant which depends on the cement type and the hardening time. From the formula is 
it easy to see that a high w/c – ratio leads to a low compressive strength (Aalborg Portland, 2010). 
As mentioned earlier it is a w/p – ratio when pozzolans, such as coal fly ash, are added to a mixture. 
This results in a small change in Bolomey’s formula.  
𝐹𝑐= 𝐾∙(
1
𝑣
𝑐+ 𝑘∙𝑃
−𝛼) 
k the powders activity factor, which describes the powders efficiency, compared to the cement, and 
P is the mass of the powder. The Bolomey formula can be used by the assumption, that concrete has 
a naturel content of air of 1%. As already mentioned does a high air content results in increased 
porosity, and thereby a lower compressive strength. A good assumption is that for each percentage 
increase in the air content, reduces the compressive strength by 4-5% (Aalborg Portland, 2010).The 
Bolomey’s formula can be rewritten to:  
𝐹𝑐= 𝐾∙(
1
𝑣
𝑐+ 𝑘∙𝑃
−𝛼) ∙(1 −0.045 ∙(𝑎−𝑎0)) 
Where a is the actual air content in the concrete and a0 is the naturel air content (about 1%). 
==PAGE:29==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
22 
 
 
 
The Bolomey formula does not include the packing of the aggregates, when calculating the 
compressive strength of the concrete. This means that two concrete mixtures with identical w/c ratio 
and air content but different aggregates size distribution, could have different compressive strength 
because of the packing effect. 
The compressive strength depends besides the air content and w/c – ratio, also on the maturity of 
the concrete. According to DS/EN 1992-1-1 is the calculations for the compressive strength in a 
concrete based on measurements, made on cylinders with a diameter of 150 mm and a height of 300 
mm, and have achieved 28 day of maturity. This strength is also known as the characteristic 
uniaxial compressive strength, fck. The compressive strength increases fast in the early hardening 
state. After just 7 days (depending on the type of cement) will the concrete have achieved about 70 
% of its design strength. After 28 days should the design strength have been achieved. Beyond the 
28 day of maturity, will the compressive strength of the concrete still increases, but this strength 
enhancement is not considered acceptable in the concrete strength according to DS/EN 1992-1-1.  
The concrete has also a tensile strength, but this is lower than the compressive strength, only about 
5-10 % of the compressive strength (Jensen, 2008). The master thesis does not investigate the 
tensile strength, but focus on the compressive strength.  
2.5 Degree of hydration 
When considering how fast the concrete is hardening, the degree of hydration is often mentioned 
because it is an expression for this process. It is defined as the weight percent of the cement, which 
is hydrated. The degree of hydration is dimensionless and is in the range of 0-1. The degree of 
hydration can be determined by measuring the development in some of the concrete properties such 
as chemical shrinkage, heat development or by measuring the chemically bound water. This master 
thesis uses the last option in order to determine the degree of hydration. The method for 
determining the chemically bound water was used by T. C. Powers in 1948, in order to make an 
empirical model for hardening of the cement paste phase distribution. His results showed that 0.23 
gram water per gram reacted cement is chemically bound (Hansen, 2008). 
 
 
==PAGE:30==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
23 
 
 
 
2.6 Packing 
In concrete technology is it known, that the grading of aggregate is one of the main factors for the 
hardened concrete strength (Neville, 1981). Aggregates are added to concrete for several reasons as 
higher strength, better durability and the fact that aggregate are cheaper than cement paste. The best 
concrete is obtained when adding as much aggregate as possible into the cement paste, however the 
rheological properties as the viscosity and the yield stress are increasing when the amount of 
particles in the concrete are increased. This means that the workability is reduced when adding 
particles, and the consequence is that the amount of particles has to be optimized, so that the 
concrete meets the requirement for transporting, placing and finishing. It is known that mixtures of 
aggregate with different size give less porous packing, than mixtures with aggregate of the same 
size and this is among other things the reasons why concrete with aggregate of different sizes have 
been used for a very long time to produce granular packing of maximal compactness. When the 
particle size distribution that gives the highest packing density is found, is the most flowable 
suspension also found as the particle volume fraction is prescribed (Chateau, 2011). 
It is difficult to predict the packing of a concrete mixture including the aggregate shape, size 
distribution and container geometry. The concrete department of Technological Institute in 
Denmark, has developed a program called 4C-Packing which can provide the packing of a mixture 
with up to 6 components including the large aggregate and the powder mass composition. The 
program takes the shape and size distribution into account. The program gives the user the 
possibility to adjust the mixture with respect to a chosen ideal particle distribution (Mathiesen, 
2001). Experiments have been made with milled SSA in mortar (Donatello, Freeman, Tyrer & 
Cheeseman, 2009). The results showed a higher compressive strength for the samples with milled 
SSA than for the reference samples without. According to the article could this result be caused by 
several reasons: a reduced water requirement, the milled material has a larger amorphous silica 
surface area and thereby a larger pozzolanic reactivity, the fine milled ash where acting as a filler or 
a combination of these. One of the reasons stated in the article Effect of milling and acid washing on 
the pozzolanic activity of incinerator were that the ash was acting as a filler and thereby improved 
the grading of the aggregate in the mortar.   
 
 
==PAGE:31==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
24 
 
 
 
2.7 Setting time 
The concrete is setting between the casting time and the hardening face. The setting time is a 
measure for how fast cement performs face changes. According to Aalborg Portland is Aalborg 
Portland Basis cement final set after 2 hours and 45 minutes. During the setting time the concrete is 
going from a liquid to a solid face, but it does not develop any strength before the hardening face. In 
order to retard or accelerate the setting time, different types of admixtures can be added to the 
concrete mixture. The  initial setting time for a concrete with 25 % coal fly ash and 75 % cement is 
according to DS/EN 450-1 not allowed to be more than double a reference concrete with 100 % 
cement. On Figure 5 below are the setting time and the beginning of the hardening face illustrated. 
It can be seen that the setting time is from one to six hours according to the cement type. The final 
setting is characterized by the unreacted cement particles and the hydration products just form a 
solid frame (Hansen, 2008). The amount of hydration products develops from one hour after casting 
and forward depending on the cement type. The setting time can be found by a manual Vicat 
apparatus or by an automatic Vicatronic. The setting time is a function of the immersion depth by a 
needle, in a cement paste sample. The friction between the needle and the cement paste increases as 
the sample sets. The initial set is defined as the time, where the needle immerses with a distance of 
6 ± 3 mm to the bottom and the final setting time is defined as the first time needle only immerses 
0,5 mm compared to the top of the sample.    
 
Figure 5 - Hydration products and process from Bygningsingeniørernes materialer - uddrag af Materialebogen. 
 
 
==PAGE:32==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
25 
 
 
 
2.8 Water requirement/additional water 
When adding water to concrete mixture, the properties of the concrete are going to change. The 
American Society for Testing and Materials (ASTM), provides in there standard ASTM 
C94/C99M, Specification for Ready Mixed Concrete, states the following regard to water additions: 
“If the desired slump or slump flow is less than specified, and unless otherwise stated, obtain the 
desired slump or slump flow within the tolerances stated in [applicable sections] with a one-time 
addition of water. Do not exceed the maximum water content for the batch as established by the 
designed mixture proportion. A one-time addition of water is not prohibited from being several 
distinct additions of water provided that no concrete has been discharged except for slump or slump 
flow testing. All water additions shall be completed within 15 min. from the start of the first water 
addition. Such additional water shall be injected into the mixer under such pressure and direction 
of flow to allow for proper distribution within the mixer. The drum shall be turned an additional 30 
revolutions, or more if necessary, at mixing speed to ensure that a homogenous mixture is 
attained.” (ASTM C94/C94M -12, “Specification for Ready Mixed Concrete”, , ASTM 
International, West Conshohocken, PA) 
 
It is common among contractors in the concrete industry to add water, to the delivered concrete 
from a concrete manufactory, before casting to increase the slump or to improve the workability of 
the concrete. By adding water is the composition of the concrete changed and the extra water has an 
influence on the concretes properties. As already mentioned would additional water increase the 
slump and improve the workability, but the extra water will change the w/c ratio and thereby have a 
negative effect on the compressive strength and other things such as the resistance to cycles of 
freezing and thawing and minimizing potential for corrosion for reinforcing steel (Cemex USA. 
2013).  
 
Because of the w/c ratios influence on the compressive strength, is the concretes water requirement 
a key factor. With a given w/c ratio, will the water requirement of the concrete have a crucial 
influence on the content of cement. The water requirement is an expression for the amount of water, 
which the concrete must contain in order to achieve the desired workability. The degree of 
plasticization, the amount of air entraining agents, the use of coal fly ash or silica fume, the biggest 
stone size and the quality of the aggregates have an influence on the water requirement. Aggregates 
with rounded edges has a lower water requirement compared to crushed or angular materials and 
the particle size distribution will also affect the water requirement in a concrete mixture. The water 
requirement will be 140-180 liter water/m3 for a concrete mixture with 16 mm stones as the biggest 
size and a slump of 100 mm, depending on the quality of the stones, sand and other aggregates 
(Aalborg Portland, 2010). 
Of the three factors, aggregates-cement ratio, w/c ratio and water content, are two of them 
independent when predicting the influence of mixture proportions on the consistency of a concrete. 
If the w/c ratio is kept constant and the aggregate-cement ratio is reduced, increases the water 
content and thereby the consistency. But if the aggregates-cement ratio is reduced and the water 
==PAGE:33==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
26 
 
 
 
content is kept constant, decreases the w/c ratio but it will not affect the consistency (Kumar Mehta 
& Monteiro, 2006) 
 
In the course, Undersøgelse af betons vandbehov ved iblanding af slamakse, was the water 
requirement for concrete with SSA tested. The bachelor projects (Carlsen & Petersen, 2013 and 
Reiff & Meldgaard, 2013) showed that mortar with SSA had an increased water requirement 
compared to the reference. It was, in the course, investigated how much extra water should be 
added to achieve the same slump as the reference. The report (Petersen & Meldgaard, 2014) 
concluded that the slump for the B samples increases with 8.2 mm per percent added extra water 
and 9.1 mm for the F samples. The report also concludes that the slump within a mixture and 
between different mixtures with the prescription is at a constant level. 
In the two bachelor projects Scanning Electron Microscope (SEM) pictures of the SSA were made, 
both the untreated and the milled. The SEM pictures, Picture 4 and Picture 45 (Carlsen & Petersen, 
2013 and Reiff & Meldgaard, 2013), shows that the untreated ash is porous, edged and bigger than 
the milled ash. The fact that the untreated ash is edged and porous could be the reason why mortar 
with SSA has an increased water requirement in order to have the same workability as the reference 
mortar.  
 
Picture 4 - Untreated SSA from Lynetten, 400 x zoom.  
Picture 5 - Milled SSA from Lynetten, 400 x zoom. 
 
   
 
 
==PAGE:34==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
27 
 
 
 
2.9 Thin section and microstructure 
The complexity of concretes microstructure makes it very difficult to predict the behavior of the 
materials. But knowledge of the microstructure along with the properties of the individual 
aggregates of the concrete and the relationship to each other is beneficial for performing controls on 
the properties. The structure is often divided into two terms, macrostructure and microstructure. The 
macrostructure is what the human eye can see, and covers therefore the gross elements of a material 
which can be seen in a cross section. The human eye has a limit of resolution for about 200 µm. The 
finer elements need to be studied by a microscope. The microstructure covers this part of the 
concrete elements, because it is a microscopically magnified portion of a macrostructure (Kumar 
Mehta & Monteiro, 2006). 
The pore structure in a concrete depends on the composition, the compacting, the mixture and the 
hardening process. With microscope analysis of thin sections, is it possible to obtain knowledge on 
how the concrete was mixed and which impacts it might have been exposed to afterwards. The thin 
section is a 20 μm thick piece of concrete with the dimension, 20 x 50 mm. The concrete becomes 
transparent at this thickness. It is necessarily to impregnate the concrete with epoxy plastic in order 
to hold the sample together during the production of the thin section. A fluorescent tracer is mixed 
into the epoxy, such as voids and crack will appear (as light) on dark background, when the thin 
section is illuminated with non-polarized light in a microscope.  
A thin section analysis, offers a qualitative evaluation of the concrete structure. Different 
characteristics of the concrete such as; the pore – and aggregate distribution, size of voids and 
crack, the porosity and the w/c ratio can be determined (Lapertis, 1999). 
 
 
==PAGE:35==
March 2015 
Theory 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
28 
 
 
 
2.10 Earlier investigations 
The result in this master thesis are based on further investigation of the results in two bachelor 
projects and a special course made at the Danish Technical University (DTU). The bachelor 
projects are called Slamaske i beton – undersøgelse af anvendelses muligheder and Slamaske i 
beton – Effekt af knusning og syrevask af slamaske and the report from the special course were 
called Undersøgelse af betons vandbehov ved iblanding af slamakse.  
The bachelor projects concluded that some of the different mortar mixtures, containing SSA, 
obtained a higher compressive strength than the reference mixture. Two of these mortar mixtures 
have been chosen for this Master thesis. The mixtures are named B and F sample. 
In the B samples are 10 % of the cement replaced with SSA and in the F samples replaces 5 % of 
the sand in the mixtures. Furthermore has the two mixtures been testes with milled SSA.  
The course was testing the concretes water requirements for the same mixtures, B and F. The report 
concluded that the slump for the B samples increases with 8.2 mm per percent added extra water 
and 9.1 mm for the F samples. The report also concludes that the slump within a mixture and 
between different mixtures with the prescription is at a constant level. The concrete consisted of 
cement (Aalborg basis), stone with a max diameter 16 mm, sand with a diameter of 0.4 mm, water 
and SSA.  
The concrete samples in this Master thesis have a different composition than in the report from the 
special course. The concrete prescription is cement, 33% stone with max diameter of 8 mm, 67 % 
stone with max diameter of 16 mm, sand with a diameter of 0.4 mm, water and SSA. Therefore has 
the theory from the special course been tested for this prescription, in order to determine the amount 
of extra water requirement when SSA is mixed into the concrete. The results from the tests are 
listed in Table 8. The listed slump values are average values made from three test for each mixture.

3.1 Characteristic of the sewage sludge ash 
3.1.1 Conductivity and pH value 
The content of ions in liquid with dissolved ash is determined by the conductivity of the ash. The 
conductivity is an expression for how fast/good an electric current is lead through a liquid, and is 
depending on the amount of conductive particles in the fluid (salt ions).  The pH value determines 
whether the ash is an alkaline or an acid environment, when it is dissolved in water. If the pH value 
for the ash is low, it could have a negative impact on the environment for the concrete mixture, 
because cement is alkaline. A low pH value could also create bigger risk for corrosion. 
3.1.2 Loss on ignition 
When a substance, such as the SSA, is placed in a furnace at 550⁰C will the LOI corresponds to the 
organic material which the substances contains. 
3.1.3 Water content 
The water content must be determine the water content in order to find out how much water is 
bound in the ash. It is important to determine this, because the amount of bound water in the ash 
could have an impact on the w/c ratio and thereby also the strength of the concrete. 
3.1.4 Water solubility 
The weight loss for the ash by filtration is determined when testing the water solubility. The water 
solubility tells about the amount of ions there are washed out, especially the amount of salts, and 
how the ash will behave when it is used in a concrete mixture. 
3.1.5 Solubilisation, metal concentration (ICP-method) 
The Danish standard, DS 259, is used to determine the metal concentration. The standard is 
developed to determine the content of acid soluble metals in a soil sample.  
3.1.6 Water soluble anions 
There are different demands for the content of anions, especially chloride, in a concrete mixture. 
The demands varies if the concrete is reinforced or not, but it is therefore important to determine the 
amount of water soluble anions in the ash, because a high level of for example chloride, could lead 
to higher risk for corrosion in reinforced concrete 
3.1.7 Grading curve 
The laboratory technicians from DTU BYG has measured the particle size distribution of the SSA 
and made grading curves. The curves will be used in order to compare the ash collected in May and 
in September.  
 
==PAGE:40==
March 2015 
Material and methods 
DTU Civil Engineering

The names for each sample describes which kind of mixture, the sample is referring to. After the 
mixture name is the curing time (ex. 28 days) and a letter (ex. A, B, C or D) given to each 
specimen. An example could be B-WO-14-A, which is a B-sample WithOut extra water, 14 days of 
maturity and cylinder A. There is no standard for mixing concrete. Each mixture has therefore 
followed this procedure: 
The compulsory mixer is started. The stone (max 16mm) is poured in, then the stone (max 8mm), 
half the sand, cement, SSA if any, then the rest of the sand and then the water. The mixer runs in 
three minutes. After this, the four cylinders are placed on a vibration table. The table is turned on 
with a frequency of 65 Hz and the cylinders are filled with concrete 1/3 of the time. The table 
vibrates for 120 seconds in total.

3.3 Compressive strength test 
Before the actual test is the dimension and weight under and above water determined. These results 
are used to determine the density of the samples, the air content and to determine the compressive 
strength. In order to determine the compressive strength, is a Toni 3000 used and the testing follows 
the demands from DS/EN 12390-3-2009. The machine presses with a constant load within the range 
4.7 KN/s ± 1.5 KN/s. The specimens are tested until they breach, and the load are notated. If a result 
is 10 % from the average values for series is this result removed, and a new average value is 
calculated with the remaining samples. This method is described in DS/EN 196-1. The compressive 
strength is then determined by using following equation from DS/EN-12390-3-2009: 
𝑓𝑐= 𝐹
𝐴𝑐
 
Where  
fc  
is the compressive strength, in MPa. 
F  
is the maximum load at failure, in N (read off from Toni 3000) 
Ac  
is the cross-section area of the specimens on which the compressive force acts.

3.4 Setting time 
A device called Vicatronic is used in order to determine the setting time for each mixture. The 
setting time is found according to the standard DS/EN 196-3 2009, were a mixture of cement, water 
and the SSA is placed in the Vicatronic. The Vicatronic makes then a measurement every 10 
minute, by dropping a needle down into the mixture. The standard can be found in appendix 2J. 
 
Picture 7 - Vicatronic. Used to determine the setting time. 
3.5 Chloride migration coefficient from non-steady-state migration experiment 
The experiment is made in order to determine the chloride migration coefficient in the concrete 
cylinders from non-steady-state migration. The experiment was made on the seven different 
concrete mixtures described in 3.2 Concrete samples. The cylinders were cut in half, and two slices 
with a thickness of 50 ± 2 mm were cut from the halves. The experiment was then carried out 
according to the Nordtest Method (NT build 492), which can be found in appendix 2I.  
  
 
Picture 8 – Experimental set-up and specimens sprayed with 0.1 M silver nitrate solution (AgNO3). 
 
 
==PAGE:43==
March 2015 
Material and methods 
DTU Civil Engineering

3.6 Degree of hydration 
Seven cement pasta samples, representing the seven mixtures in Table 10, were made in order to 
determine the degree of hydration. The mixing of water, cement and SSA follows the standard 
DS/EN 196-1-2005, just without adding any sand. The paste is then placed in moulds, sealed and 
then set to rotate for 24 hours. After 28 days of hardening the samples are ready for testing. The test 
is carried out as described in the article from Cement and Concrete, Laboratory exercises. The 
article can be seen in appendix 2H. 
 
Picture 9 - Specimens placed 1050 degree furnace. 
3.7 Pozzolanic effect 
It has also been investigated whether the SSA has a pozzolanic effect or not. A way to determine if 
the SSA has pozzolanic properties is to test the compressive strength of concrete cylinders were the 
ash substitute 25 % of the cement, according to DS/EN 450-1. The compressive strength is tested on 
cylinders were the ash was milled and untreated. The pozzolanic effect is tested on cylinders that 
have hardened for 28 days. The results are compared with reference samples.

3.8 Particle density 
As mentioned earlier in 2.10 Earlier investigations, the particle density of the SSA was found in the 
earlier studies. But when the ash is milled, the particle density changes. The test has therefore been 
made on the milled ash. The pycnometer method, which is described in DS/CEN ISO/TS 17892-3 
2004, were used in order to determine the particle density.  
 
Picture 10 - Pycnometer in an exicator. 
 
 
3.9 Absorption and saturated surface dry ash (s.s.d. ash) 
About 200 g of s.s.d. ash is needed in order to determine the absorption of the ash. One way to 
make sure that the ash is in s.s.d. mode is to take a sample (about 1000 g) of water storage ash (at 
least 24 hours), and then place it in a tray and carefully dry the ash with a hot air blower. The drying 
is stopped just before the ash particles are no longer sticking to each other. The ash is then cooled 
down. After the ash is cooled, it is placed in a cone and stamped 25 times. The cone is then lifted 
and it is evaluated whether the ash has reached s.s.d. condition or not. Figure 6 shows how to 
evaluate the ash. The figure is taken from the guidelines given in the DTU course, Laboratorie 
øvelser. 
 
Figure 6 - The four types of cones. A) Very moist B) moist C) s.s.d. condition D) to dry. 
 
 
A
B
C
D
==PAGE:45==
March 2015 
Material and methods 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
38 
 
 
 
If the ash falls like it does in illustration C, it should be in s.s.d. condition. Then is about 200 g 
weight of (the weight is notated) and placed into a heating cabinet until the samples is totally dry. 
The new weight is notated, and the absorption can be determined. The guidelines for these test and 
calculation are also from the course Laboratorie øvelser, on DTU and can be seen in appendix 2K. 
 
Picture 11 - Cone of s.s.d. conditioned ash.

4 Results 
All the results from this master thesis will be presented in this section. The SSA was collected two 
times from Lynettefællesskabet I/S, in May 2014 and in September 2014. A characteristic were 
made on both samples in order to see the difference in quality, if any. All raw data can be found in 
appendix 3A - 10. 
4.1 Characteristic 
4.1.1 Conductivity and pH 
The results regarding the conductivity are for both samples an average made on three samples. The 
results are listed in Table 11, and as it can be seen is the conductivity for Lynetten-Ny-3 and 
Lynetten-Ny-4 very similar as well as the Standard deviation. Lynetten-Ny-3 has a 4.9 percent 
higher conductivity compared to Lynetten-Ny-4.

tables. The results for the B-samples, F-samples and reference are plotted in Figure 8 to Figure 15,

4.10 Setting time 
According to 2.7 Setting time should the final setting time occur after 2 hours and 45 minutes for 
the reference sample with Aalborg Portland basis cement. The results can be seen in Figure 18 and 
Figure 19 and the final setting time for the reference sample is verified as it sets at the 170th minute. 
Both the initial and final setting time is increased for the B-samples. The F-samples have similar 
setting times as the reference sample, both initial and final. The reasons why the horizontal 
asymptote are higher for the reference samples compared to rest is that the cement pastes layer 
thickness was about 3-5 mm thicker than for the rest. This should not have any effect on the final 
result of the tests.

As mentioned in 2.7 Setting time has DS/EN 450-1 a requirement for concrete containing coal fly 
ash. The requirement is that concrete with 25% coal fly ash and 75% cement are not allowed to 
have more than doubled the initial setting time compared to a concrete with 100% cement. In order 
to control whether the SSA meets the requirement stated in DS/EN 450-1 were the Vicatronic tests 
made on paste sample containing 25% untreated SSA and 25% milled SSA. The results are plotted 
in Figure 20 and shows that the initial setting time for the untreated SSA is about three times longer 
than the reference. The paste with milled SSA has on the other hand an initial setting time which is 
only about 6 minutes longer than the reference and thereby meets the requirement.

5 Discussion 
In this section is all the results discussed and the two ashes, Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 are 
compared. A comparison of the SSA is also made with previous results from a bachelor project 
(Reiff & Meldgaard, 2013).  
5.1 Characteristic 
The results from Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 regarding conductivity, pH,  LOI, water 
content, water solubility, solubilisation and water soluble anions are very similar as seen in 4.1 
Characteristic. There are no significant different between the characteristic results from Lynetten-
Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4, which indicates that the incineration and the waste water treated in the 
plant has been similar. The biggest difference in the results is for the LOI and the water content. 
This difference can be explained by one value that is significant higher than the rest in both cases 
and this also explains the difference in the standard deviations. It can be seen that the average pH 
value is about 12.5, which places the ash in the alkaline area on the pH-scale just as cement.  
The results regarding the characteristic of the SSA should be comparable with Lynetten-Ny-1 but 
there are significant differences in the results regarding conductivity, LOI, water content and water 
solubility. As seen in Table 7  is the conductivity for Lynetten-Ny-1 7.81 mS/cm which is 16.5 
percent smaller than the average value for Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 which is 9.36 mS/cm. 
This could be because of a higher content of soluble anions for Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 
compared to Lynetten-Ny-1. The amount of chloride ions in Lynetten-Ny-1 is 96.53 mg/kg (Reiff & 
Meldgaard, 2013) and the average amount of chloride ions in Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 is 
137.38 mg/kg which is about 42 percent higher than Lynetten-Ny-1, and therefore this could be one 
of the reasons for a higher conductivity.  
The average value for Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 is 0.12 percent for the results regarding 
the LOI. This places the SSA in category A (less than 5%) according to Table 2. It is beneficial to 
use a product with a low LOI because the amount of unburnt carbon could have an influence on the 
effect of the air-entraining agents used in a concrete mix. When the result for LOI is compared to 
previously result for Lynetten-Ny-1 Table 7 it can be seen that the average value for Lynetten-Ny-3 
and Lynetten-Ny-4 is 46.6 percent smaller than Lynetten-Ny-1. This could indicate that 
Lynettefælledsskabet I/s have improved their incineration.  
The biggest difference between Lynetten-Ny-1, Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 is the water 
content. The average value for Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 is 0.22 percent which is 146 
percent higher than for Lynetten-Ny-1 (Reiff & Meldgaard, 2013). The SSA should not contain any 
water after incineration, and this means that the water present when tested is absorbed from the 
surrounding air. The difference between Lynetten-Ny-1 and the average value for Lynetten-Ny-3 
and Lynetten-Ny-4 can therefore be explained by the way the ash has been stored, transported and 
for how long time the ash has been exposed to air. There is no requirement in DS/EN 450-1 
regarding the water content, but it is an important factor for making the proportions concrete and 
determine the w/c – ratio. 
==PAGE:68==
March 2015 
Discussion 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
61 
 
 
 
When the water solubility results from Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 are compared to 
previously found data, it can be seen that Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 have relatively low 
water solubility. Lynetten-Ny-1 had a water solubility of 5.12 % (ref, table theory) and another SSA 
from Avedøre-Ny-2 had a water solubility of 29.5 % (Kurt, 2014). The water solubility is 
determined by the amount of water soluble anions such as chloride, sulfate and nitrate. These anions 
are not beneficial to have in a concrete because they can react with calcium in the cement and form 
calcium sulfate and calcium nitrate. When the calcium is bound to the sulfate or nitrate can it not 
react with the water and form the hydration products. This means that if the calcium reacts with the 
anions are it not available in the hydration process and the final concrete will not obtain the design 
strength. Therefore is it beneficial to have a low water solubility.  
5.2 Heavy metals and anions 
When the results from solubilisation tests, Table 16, are compared it can be seen that the 
concentration of metals are similar to each other. The averages values for each metal concentration 
from Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 are compared with previously results (Reiff & Meldgaard, 
2013) there is a difference between the values. There are no significant changes in the way the 
results are different from each other. Some of the heavy metals are increasing in the concentration 
and some are decreasing. The biggest difference between the average value from Lynetten-Ny-3 
and Lynetten-Ny-4 and the concentration of heavy metals from Lynetten-Ny-1 is for lead, chrome 
and nickel. The concentration of lead in Lyntten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 is about three times 
higher than for Lynetten-Ny-1 and the concentration of chrome and nickel in Lynetten-Ny-3 and 
Lynetten-Ny-4 is about one and half times higher than for Lynetten-Ny-1.  
This difference is due to the natural variation in the waste water composition, but shows that it is 
necessary to measure the concentration of the heavy metals for a longer period, in order to 
determine the variations. When the variations of the concentration of heavy metals are so 
significant, even though the SSA comes from the same plant, is it incorrect to say that all sewage 
sludge ashes are the same. The final product from the plant, SSA, is depending on the catchment 
area and the way the waste water is treated at the plant. The fact that no plants have the exact same 
treatment and catchment area means that no ashes are identical, and it is therefore necessary to 
investigate the SSA before using it for other purposes.  
As mentioned in 2.3.4 Heavy metals provides the Danish Ministry of Environment two notices with 
limit values for the concentration of heavy metals. When the sewages sludge ash from Lynetten-Ny-
3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 is compared with the limit values from Slambekendtgørelsen, it is shown that 
the ashes contains too much cadmium, nickel and lead. This means that the SSA do not comply 
with the notice and can therefore not be used for agricultural purposes. In order to use the ash must 
it be treated in some way to decreases the concentration of heavy metals. The values for Lynetten-
Ny-3, Lynetten-Ny -4 and the limit values from (Miljøstyrelsen, December  2006) are listed in 
Table 27. The red numbers shows the samples where the limit values are exceeded and the green 
numbers shows the samples which meets the requirements.

As mentioned in 2.3.4 Heavy metals is the concentration of phosphorous in general about 5-10 % 
per weight in SSA. This is also the case for Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 which has a 
concentration about 10 % according to Table 17. When these results are compared with the 
concentration of phosphorous from Lynetten-Ny-1, which is about 16 % (Reiff & Meldgaard, 
2013), it could indicate that the SSA from Lynetten has a concentration of phosphorous in the upper 
range or above. A high amount of phosphorous in concrete can retard the hydration processes of the 
cement and thereby reduce the strength development (Hasholt & Mathiesen, 2002). It is therefore 
beneficial to extract the phosphorous from the SSA before use, and thereby use the ash as a 
secondary resource for phosphorous 2.3.4 Heavy metals.  
 
The concentration of chlorides in Lynetten-Ny-1 is 96.53 mg/kg which is 42 percent smaller than 
the average concentration 137.37 mg/kg for Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4. Just as for the 
heavy metals this difference can be explained by the variation of waste water that the plant is 
cleansing. The average value also meets the requirement for the amount of chlorides in the ash 
provide by DS/EN 450-1 listed in Table 2 (0.013% < 0.1%). As mentioned in 2.3.1 Chlorides the 
amount of chloride present in concrete construction can course damage to the reinforcement. The 
proportions of chloride relative to the amount of cement is therefore calculative for each mixture 
tested in this master thesis, in order to control if the requirements listed in Table 3 is met. The 
amount of chloride per kg SSA is highest for Lynetten-Ny-3 compared with Lynetten-Ny-4. The 
calculations are therefore made for Lynetten-Ny-3 and if this meets the requirements so does 
Lynetten-Ny-4.  The results are listed in Table 30, and shows that all the concrete mixtures meet all 
the requirements, because the largest proportion of chloride is calculated to 0.0017 % and this value 
is way below the requirement interval 0.1-1.0 % chloride. This means that the SSA from Lynetten-
Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 can be used with these seven different mixture types in concrete 
constructions without risking corrosion of the reinforcement.
