5.3 Chloride migration coefficient and w/c - ratio 
As seen in Table 26 and Figure 17 has the reference sample a chloride migration coefficient of 16.2 
* 10-12 m2/s. All the B-samples have a higher chloride migration coefficient than the reference and 
all the F-samples have a lower chloride migration coefficient than the reference. This shows that the 
B-samples are not as dense as the reference, and that the transport of chlorides ions happens easier 
in the B-samples compared with the reference. The results for the F-samples show the opposite 
tendency that the F-samples are denser than the reference and that the transport of chloride ions 
therefore is slowed down.  
When these results are compared with the calculation for the w/c – and w/p- ratios it can be seen 
that there is a tendency to a high w/c – and w/p- ratios give a high chloride migration coefficient, 
meaning that the transport of chloride ions can happen easier. According to 2.3.2 Environmental 
classes is a concrete with a high compressive strength chosen when the concrete is exposed to an 
aggressive environment. As mentioned in 2.4 Strength leads a low w/c – ratio to a high compressive 
strength. Therefore are the B-samples with a high w/c –ratio and high chloride migration coefficient 
less suitable than the F-sample because the transport of chloride ions happens easier and thereby 
can damage the reinforcement in a concrete construction.  
It was expected that the WO (WithOut extra water) samples would have a lower chloride migration 
coefficient compared to the W (With extra water) samples, because of the different w/c – ratios. As 
seen in Table 26 and Figure 17 this is also the case, but the tendency is most evident with the B-
samples where the B-WO samples are about 7 % smaller than the B-W samples and the F-WO is 
only about 2 % smaller than the F-W samples. It was also expected that the M (Milled ash) samples 
would have the same or a lower chloride migration coefficient compared to the WO samples. This 
is due to the fact that the milled ash should tribute to a more densely packed concrete mixture. 
However the results are not consistent as seen in Table 26 and Figure 17. The results for the B-M 
samples is about 8 % higher than the B-WO samples and the F-M samples are about 3 % lower than 
the F-WO samples. The fact that the B-M samples have a higher chloride migration coefficient than 
the B-WO sample could be that the pore size distribution is different. Because of the smaller 
particles could there be more small pores acting like capillary pore, which could draw the chloride 
ions into the concrete.    
 
 
==PAGE:74==
March 2015 
Discussion 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
67 
 
 
 
5.4 Setting time 
As mentioned in 4.10 Setting time is the initial and final setting time increased for the B-sample 
compared to the reference but it is just a few minutes. The results for the F-samples showed that the 
initial and final setting time were the same as for the reference. Therefore there are no significant 
changes in the setting time when using the SSA in the amount as in the 6 different mixtures for B 
and F.   
For the test made on paste containing 25 % untreated SSA and 75 % cement were the initial setting 
time about three times as long as the reference, and the final setting time does not seem to be reach 
within the 860 minute  test. One of the physical requirements from DS/EN 450-1 for coal fly ash is 
that a concrete with 25 % ash and 75 % cement must not have an initial setting time which is more 
than double for a reference concrete with 100 % cement. The untreated ash does not meet this 
requirement but the milled ash does. The initial setting time for the paste with milled ash is 6 
minutes longer than the reference. This could indicated that the milled ash have some pozzolanic 
properties which the untreated ash does not have in the same amount. The reasons why the milled 
ash has a more present pozzolanic activity than the untreated ash, could be that the area where the 
hydration processes takes placed becomes larger when the ash is milled. 
 
 
==PAGE:75==
March 2015 
Discussion 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
68 
 
 
 
5.5 Air content 
As mentioned in 4.6 Air content were the aggregates particle densities adjusted so that the air 
content in the reference sample becomes 0 %. The actual air content for each mixture is therefore 
not determined, but the changes in the amount of air in the different mixtures are studied. In order to 
show the changes in the air content is the average value from Table 23 and the standard deviation 
plotted in Figure 21. It can be seen that the air content is larger in all the 6 mixtures containing SSA 
compared to the reference. According to 2.4 Strength is the compressive strength reduced by 4-5 % 
when the air content is increased with 1 %. It is therefore, based on the results shown in Figure 21, 
expected that the B and F-sample will obtain a lower compressive strength than the reference. The 
vibration times were according to 3.2 Concrete samples 120 seconds for all the mixtures. There is a 
tendency to a higher air content in the samples without extra water, and the lowest air content is in 
the samples with extra water. This means that there is a correlation between the air content and the 
amount of water/slump value. A higher slump value makes it easier for the air to be vibrated out of 
the concrete. According to appendix 10 was the slump value for the M samples equal to the slump 
value for the reference and W samples. However the M samples have a higher air content than both 
the reference and W samples.  
It was noticed during the vibration that the M samples were more difficult to vibrate, because they 
seemed to have a lower workability than the rest, even though that they have the same slump value. 
It seems like the concrete with milled ash is more cohesive than the rest of the mixtures. A cohesive 
concrete mixture is in general more able to hold air than either a very wet or a very stiff mixture 
(Kumar Mehta & Monteiro, 2006). If a concrete is going to be frost resistance must the actual air 
content be at least 4.5 % 2.1 Concrete and the air voids must have a certain size, number and 
spacing (Kumar Mehta & Monteiro, 2006).

5.6 Degree of hydration 
As seen in Table 25 have all the six samples which contains SSA, a lower degree of hydration 
compared to the reference sample. The degree of hydration is depending on the amount of free 
water present in the concrete. Therefore should the samples with extra water (W) have a higher 
degree of hydration than the samples without extra water (WO). This is also the case for the B-
samples. A reason why the B-M samples with milled ash obtains the same degree of hydration as 
the B-W samples, could be because there is a larger area in which the reactions can take place, 
when the ash is milled. But when looking at the F-samples this is not the case. The samples without 
extra water (WO) have now the highest degree of hydration and the samples with milled ash (M) 
have a lower degree of hydration than the samples with extra water (W). The results are plotted in 
Figure 22 along with the maximum and minimum values for each sample. 
Normally would it be expected that the degree of hydration was about 0.7 after 28 days of maturity, 
which is significant higher than the results indicates. The reason for the lower value could be that 
the tests are made with Aalborg Portland basic cement, which contains 15 % filler which does not 
react. This means that the degree of hydration would be 0.85 when the cement paste is fully 
hydrated.

5.7 Grading curves 
The particles size distributions for Lynetten-Ny-3 and Lynetten-Ny-4 was very similar as expected, 
see Figure 7. According to (Kappel, 2015) is the fineness of the SSA increases when milling the 
ash. A positive effect is seen on the compressive strength of concrete with SSA when the ash has 
been milled before used in the concrete mix design. According to (Kappel, 2015) is the compressive 
strength increasing along with the time the ash is milled. From somewhere between 30 seconds and 
3 minutes is maximum effect seen and in this master thesis is the ash milled for 30 seconds in order 
to utilize the time in the best way. All the grading curves can be seen appendix 3I.

5.8 Compressive strength 
The amounts of concrete samples which are sufficient for making an appropriate average value for 
the compressive strength were investigated. The results from six references samples, casted on two 
different days (three on each day), are compared. The compressive strength are listed and plotted in 
Table 31 and Figure 23. The three blue columns represent the compressive strength for the samples 
cast 13-11-2014 and the three red columns represent the samples cast 17-11-2014. As it can be seen 
the results for each day are very similar. Based on these results it is concluded that three samples 
are sufficient in order to see tendencies and thereby be able to investigate wider.

almost identical at early age. This shows that the milled ash does not have any influence on the

compressive strength after 140 days of maturity, but has the lowest at 42 days of maturity. The 
strength development for the reference, B-WO and B-W samples are almost identical. The only 
sample which does not have the same strength development is the B-M sample, which has a much 
higher strength development from 42 to 140 days of maturity compared to the others. This could 
indicate that the milled ash has an influence on the late strength development compared to the 
untreated ash.  
When looking at Figure 8 and Figure 9 the compressive strength is decreasing from 5 to 7 days of 
maturity and again from 28 to 42 days of maturity. The strength for a concrete should not decrease 
over time 2.4 Strength. New 7 and 42 days samples were casted and tested in order to verify the 
theory that the strength should increase over time. When looking at Figure 10 and Figure 11 it can 
be seen that all the new samples reaches a higher compressive strength compared to the old 
samples. The decrease at 7 and 42 day of maturity does not happen with the new samples and the B-
M sample goes from having the lowest compressive strength at 42 days of maturity to have a higher 
strength than B-WO and B-W. The milled ash seems to influence the late strength development 
from 28 to 140 days of maturity according to Figure 11. It can be seen that the B-M sample has the 
lowest compressive strength at 28 days of maturity but the highest at 140 days.  
In order to control whether the strength development is the same for mortar samples and concrete 
samples is the compressive strength results for the B-samples compared with mortar samples (Reiff 
& Meldgaard, 2013) with the same recipe and cast with the same kind of SSA. The compressive 
strengths are compared after 7 and 28 days of maturity and plotted in Figure 24 and Figure 25. After 
7 days of maturity is there coherence between the compressive strength for mortar and concrete 
samples. The B-M concrete and the reference concrete have the same strength (30 MPa) and 
likewise has the MB mortar and the reference mortar the same strength (48 MPa). The samples with 
the untreated ash, B-WO and B-W, have for both mortar and concrete a lower compressive strength 
than the others. At 7 days of maturity is there a tendency that the results from mortar tests can be 
transferred directly to concrete.  
When looking at the results after 28 days of maturity, there is not the same tendency as for the 7 day 
samples. The MB mortar and the reference mortar have the same strength (57 MPa) but the B-M 
concrete and the reference concrete has not the same strength (35 MPa and 40 MPa). The samples 
with the untreated ash, B-WO and B, have for both mortar and concrete a lower compressive 
strength than the reference just as for the 7 day samples. The reason why the B-M concrete has a 
lower compressive strength after 28 days of maturity compared to the reference could be the 
aforementioned late strength development which the milled ash could have when mix into concrete. 
This indicate that when the milled ash is mixed into mortar is the strength development more rapid 
than when the milled ash is mixed into concrete, because the concrete contain the milled ash not 
achieve the same compressive strength until after about 100 days of maturity as seen in Figure 11.

When comparing the first casted F-samples at an early age (1, 3, 5 and 7 days of maturity) Figure

the F-WO and the F-M samples were expected to be about the same at early age, just as for the B-

lower strength at 42 days of maturity compared to the reference.

maturity and again from 28 to 42 days of maturity. As for the B-samples should the strength for a 
concrete not decrease with time 2.4 Strength. New 7 and 42 days samples were casted and tested in 
order to verify the theory that the strength should increase over time. When looking at Figure 14 
and Figure 15 it can be seen that all the new samples again reaches a higher compressive strength 
compared to the old samples. The decrease at 7 and 42 day of maturity does not happen with the 
new samples and the F-WO sample reaches the same compressive strength at 42 days of maturity as 
the reference. The milled ash seems to influence the late strength development from 42 to 140 days 
of maturity according to Figure 15. It can be seen that the F-M sample has the lowest compressive 
strength at 42 days of maturity but the highest at 140 days.  
The results for the B and F-samples indicate that the milled SSA tribute to the compressive strength 
of concrete, especially beyond 28-42 days of maturity just as coal fly ash 2.1.2 Coal fly ash.

A different vibration table, small vibration table, was used to the old samples with 7 and 42 days of 
maturity compared to the other samples, which were vibrated on a large vibration table. In order to 
compare the two vibration methods are the results plotted against each other in Figure 26 and Figure 
27. As shown reaches the samples vibrated at the large vibration table the highest strength for all the 
samples. The largest difference is seen in the 42 days of maturity, Figure 27. This shows the 
importance of using equipment with the same properties if the results should be comparable.

Discussion 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
76 
 
 
 
As mentioned in 3.7 Pozzolanic effect has the pozzolanic effect of the SSA been tested. Based on 
these tests must the samples containing 25 % SSA obtain 75 % of the compressive strength for a 
reference in order to have a pozzolanic effect. As shown in Figure 16 reaches the sample with the 
untreated ash 76 % of the strength of the reference and the sample containing milled ash reach 78 % 
of the reference strength. These results indicate that the SSA could have some pozzolanic activity 
and the pozzolanic effect seems to be more pronounced when the ash is milled. These results are 
supported by the results regarding the setting times, because when the sample containing 25 % 
milled ash had almost the same setting time as the reference, where the sample with 25 untreated 
ash had about three time as long setting time as the reference. This indicates that the setting process 
is speeded up when milled ash is added to a mixture, due to a possible pozzolanic activity. When 
the SSA replaces 25 % of cement is it observed that the paste in the concrete sticks to the lid of the 
cylinder form. It is not known why the paste sticks to the lid, but it could be because the SSA 
causes a reaction between the metal and the concrete or that the binding between the paste and the 
larger aggregates is weaker than the binding between the lid and paste. When the paste sticks to the 
lid it gives an uneven surface as seen in Picture 27, which can affect the result regarding the 
compressive strength. Therefore were a wooden fiber plate used on the top and bottom of the 
cylinder in order to provide an even pressure area for all the cylinders. 
 
Picture 27 - Uneven surface on samples used for pozzolanic effect tests. 
According to Bolomey’s formula should the compressive strength for a reference sample cast with 
Aalborg Portland basis cement have a compressive strength of 42.7 MPa after 28 days of maturity. 
In order to calculate the activity index for the untreated and milled SSA is the 76 % and 78 % of the 
compressive strength for the reference found. The compressive strengths are respectively 32.4 MPa 
and 33.3 MPa. This provides the basis for the activity index calculations.

This is a calculation which provides an indication of the activity index value. The activity index for 
the SSA is less than for cement (1), coal fly ash (0.5) and silica fume (2). If the activity index could 
be calculated after longer time than 28 days of maturity, could this give a higher value due to the 
later strength development which the ash seems to provide.  
In order to control the correlation between the calculated w/c and w/p-ratios and the compressive 
strength are these plotted in Figure 28 and Figure 29, for the samples with 28 days of maturity. As 
seen in Figure 28 is there a correlation between a low w/c – ratio and a high compressive strength 
for the B-samples. The reference sample has the lowest w/c – ratio and the highest compressive 
strength. As seen in Figure 29 is there not the same correlation between the w/p – ratio and the 
compressive strength for the F-samples. The F-Wo and F-W have a lower w/p – ratio than the 
reference and also a lower compressive strength.

The w/c – ratio varies due to the different water content in the aggregate on different casting days, 
see Table 19 and Table 20. The difference in water content in the aggregate has an effect on the 
results for the strength, 2.4 Strength. According to Bolomey’s formula is the compressive strength, 
after 28 days of maturity, for the reference with the highest and lowest w/c – ratio (0.54 and 0.51) 
respectively 43.8 MPa and 40.6 MPa. This means that the different water content in the aggregates 
could give a variation of about 8 % for the resultant compressive strength. In order to control the 
strength of the concrete it is therefore necessary to determine the water content in the aggregates 
before casting, in order to take this into account in the mix design.  
5.9 Colour of the concrete 
The SSA from Lynetten has a red/brown colour which also colours the concrete. The amount of ash 
in the concrete determines how red/brown the samples are going to be. However this colour would 
in many places not be a problem, because the concrete often would be covered by other materials. 
The red/brown colour fades over time, and it is therefore important to inform the change in colour 
to the customers. Just as many other stone types are there a difference in the colour depending on 
whether the concrete are wet or dry. The difference in colour for the seven concrete types with wet 
or dry surface can be seen in Picture 28 and in Picture 29. The concrete samples on the pictures are 
about four months old. 
 
Picture 28 - Difference in colour, dry surface. 
 
Picture 29 - Difference in colour, wet surface. 
 
 
==PAGE:86==

Discussion 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
79 
 
 
 
5.10 Thin section 
The thin section analysis was, as mentioned in 3.10 Thin section, performed in collaboration with 
Kirsten Eriksen, expert in concrete technology at COWI A/S. By analyzing the different thin 
sections for all the different mixtures was Kirsten able estimate the w/c – ratio to 0.5-0.6 which it 
verified by the result in Table 20. The analysis showed that the mixing process of the concrete had 
been good, because there were only a small amount of air and the different mixtures were 
homogenous or slightly in-homogenous, see Picture 30. The homogeneity is shown by the 
difference in brightness on Picture 30. The more bright the cement paste looks, the higher is the w/c 
– ratio in this region of the paste. The analysis showed also that the ash is distributed evenly over 
the cross section and does not clumps together as seen in Picture 31, and some of the untreated SSA 
particles were porous, see Picture 31. A concern about milling the ash was that this could lead to 
clumps, but this is not the case. As seen in Picture 32 is the ash still distributed evenly over the 
cross section, the size has been reduced and it looks as if there are no more porous ash particle when 
the ash is milled. By the results from the analysis it cannot be determined whether the ash hydrates 
or not. As seen in Picture 33, the concrete had maybe been vibrated for too long, because bleeding 
occurs around some of the aggregates. As seen in Picture 30 contains the aggregates used for the 
concrete reactive flint (blue colour) which could lead to ASR. The analysis showed that Aalborg 
Portland basis cement not was suitable for concrete used for thin section analysis. Aalborg Portland 
basis cement contains 15 % filler, which blurs the images when looking at the microstructure of the 
concrete. The filler can be seen in Picture 34. According to Kirsten Eriksen should a low alkali 
cement type be used when the concrete are going to be analyzed in a microscope, in order to get a 
clear image of the microstructure.

Conclusion 
DTU Civil Engineering 
 
 
 
81 
 
 
 
6 Conclusion 
From the results regarding the characteristic of the sewage sludge ash and the comparison with 
previous results with ash from Lynetten, it can be concluded that it is very important to determine 
the characteristic of the actual ash which is investigated, because the properties are often very 
different. The sewage sludge ash investigated in this project contains a number of heavy metals, of 
which some are in concentrations exceeding the limit values from the Danish Ministry of 
Environment. There is about 10 % phosphorous in the ash which along with the heavy metals can 
be extracted and thereby use the ash as a secondary resource for these chemical elements.  
A correlation is found between the w/c-ratio or w/p-ratio and the chloride migration coefficient. A 
high w/c – ratio leads to a high chloride migration coefficient. The samples where 5 % sand was 
replaced with sewage sludge ash (F-samples), were more dense than the samples where 10 % 
cement was replaced with sewage sludge ash (B-samples), and the reference, and are thereby more 
suitable in an aggressive environment. The samples with sewage sludge ash contain more air than 
the reference sample. The samples with the lowest chloride migration coefficient have the highest 
air content. This could be beneficial if a concrete with sewage sludge ash were placed in an 
aggressive environment with both chloride and frost attack.  
The initial and final setting times for the B and F-samples were not significantly different from the 
reference. When the sewage sludge ash replaced 25 % of the cement the initial setting was three 
times longer for the sample with untreated ash compared to the reference where the sample with 
milled ash had the same initial setting time. This indicates that the milled ash has a pozzlanic 
activity which the untreated do not have in the same way. The results from the pozzolanic effect 
tests support the setting time results because the samples with milled ash obtain a higher 
compressive strength than the sample with untreated ash.  
When looking at strength development for the B-samples can it be seen that the milled ash seems to 
influence the late strength development from 28 to 140 days of maturity. It can be seen that the B-M 
sample has the lowest compressive strength at 28 days of maturity but the highest at 140 days. The 
milled ash influences also the late strength development for the F-samples from 42 to 140 days of 
maturity. It can be seen that the F-M sample has the lowest compressive strength at 42 days of 
maturity but the highest at 140 days. The results from the degree of hydrations test shows that the 
samples contain sewage sludge ash have a lower degree of hydration than the reference after 28 
days of maturity, which also indicates that the reference sample has a faster strength development 
up till 28 days of maturity compared with the samples containing sewage sludge ash. 
Based on the results from the characterization of the sewage sludge ash, chloride migration 
coefficient, setting time, pozzolanic effect and compressive strength tests can it be concluded that 
the sewage sludge ash from Lynetten is suitable as partly replacement of sand or cement in a 
concrete mix in the same amount as for the B and F-samples. Concrete containing Lynetten sewage 
sludge ash could be suitable in constructions, which are not depending on the early strength.

Appendix 2A - pH 
 
 
 
12 
 
pH i 1 M KCl opslemning. Jord-væske forhold 1:2,5 
 
 
A 
Princip 
 
Jordens pH måles potentiometrisk i en opslemning af jord og kaliumchlorid i 
forholdet 1:2,5 
 
 
 
B 
Specielt apparatur 
 
Til målingen benyttes kombinationsselektrode i forbindelse med et pH-meter, der 
tillader en nøjagtighed på 0,05 pH-enheder. 
 
 
 
C 
Kemikalie sikkerhed 
 
Kaliumchlorid - Der foreligger ikke information, der gør det muligt at 
klassificere stoffet. Bruges med omtanke. 
 
Læs kemikaliebrugsanvisningen før arbejdet begynder. 
 
 
 
D 
Reagenser 
 
1) 
Kaliumchlorid 1 M KCl: 
74,56 g KCl afvejes på teknisk vægt og opløses i destilleret vand i en 1000,00 mL 
målekolbe. 
 
2) 
Bufferopløsning, pH 4,01: 
Radiometer pH 4. 
 
3)

Radiometer pH 7.

Appendix 2A - pH 
 
 
 
22 
E 
Analysens udførelse 
 
1) 
Fremstilling af jordopslemningen 
5,00 g tør, knust jord afvejes på teknisk vægt i en 20 mL plastvials. Der tilsættes 
12,5 mL 1 M KCl-opløsning med pipette. Opslemningen stilles på rystebord i 1 
time, hvorefter jorden bundfældes i 5-10 minutter, indtil en væskefase har udskilt 
sig over jorden. 
 
2) 
Målingens udførelse 
Elektroden anbringes i den øvre kolloidfattige del af opslemningen således at 
diafragmaet er under væskeoverfladen (under måling skal gummiproppen være 
aftaget). Undgå at elektroden ikke rammer jorden. Forud for hver måling skylles 
elektroden grundigt med destilleret vand, hvorefter en evt. dråbe aftørres 
forsigtigt med et stykke papir.  
 
 
 
F 
Beregning af resultat 
 
Resultatet aflæses direkte på apparatet og angives som pH (KCl) med en 
decimal. Aflæsningen anses for stabil når der står “STAB” på displayet. 
 
 
 
G 
Affaldshåndtering 
 
Kaliumchlorid og Metrohm pH 4 og pH 7 hældes i vasken. 
 
Plastvial med jorden/KCl skal opsamles i beholder til jordaffald.

Appendix 2B – Conductivity  
 
 
12 
 
Måling af ledningsevne i jord 
 
 
A 
Princip 
 
En jordvæskes indhold af ioner og opløste forbindelser er af stor interesse, da 
dette kan give et billede af en jordvæskes samlede koncentration (ionstyrke), 
ionernes aktivitetsforhold i væsken samt give et indtryk af ligevægtsforholdene 
mellem faste bestanddele og opløste forbindelser. Ledningsevnen for en 
jordvæske er et udtryk for, hvor godt en elektrisk strøm kan ledes gennem den 
givne væske og den afhænger af, hvor mange lede partikler, der er til stede i 
væsken. 
 
 
 
B 
Specielt apparatur 
 
Til målingen benyttes ledningsevnemåler. 
 
 
 
C 
Analysens udførelse 
 
10,00 g tør knust jord afvejes på teknisk vægt til en 50 mL plastikflaske.  Der 
tilsættes 25,00 mL destilleret vand med fuldpipette. Plastikflaskerne stilles ca. ½ 
time på rysteapparat, hvorefter jorden bundfældes i ca. 20 minutter, indtil en 
væskefase har udskilt sig over jorden.  
 
Ledningsevneelektroden skylles med destilleret vand. Sæt elektroden ned i 
væske og der trykkes på “SAMPLE”. Når displayet viser “STAB” tryk på “PRINT” 
og aflæs resultatet.

Appendix 2C – Loss on ignition 
 
 
11 
 
Glødetab i jord 
 
 
A 
Princip 
 
Jordens indhold af organisk materiale svarer stort set til det opnåede 
glødetab ved 550C. I en tungmetalforurenet jord vil tungmetallerne i høj 
grad reagere med det organiske materiale i jorden og sidder bundet i 
denne fraktion. 
 
B 
Specielt apparatur 
 
Til analysen benyttes et muffelovn. 
 
 
C 
Analysens udførelse 
 
3 digler sættes i muffelovn, som er opvarmet til 550C, hvor de står i 30 
minutter (1time hvis ovnen er kold). Diglerne afkøles i ekssikkator og 
vejes på analyse vægt.  
 
NB: Diglerne transporteres med tang, da fingrene afsætter fugt 
og derved ændrer vægten. 
 
Derefter afvejes omkring 2,5 g tør, knust jord på analysevægt til hver 
digel. Diglerne med den tørre jord stilles i en opvarmet muffelovn og 
glødes ved 550C i 1 time. 
 
Diglerne med den glødede jord afkøles i ekssikkator og vejes på analyse 
vægt.  
 
 
 
D 
Beregning af resultat 
 
Glødetabet, som nogenlunde svarer til prøvens indhold af organisk stof, 
angives i procent af tørstofindholdet.

Appendix 2D – Water solubility 
Vandopløselighed 
 
Analyse: 
 
100,00 g tørret aske afvejes på teknisk vægt til en 1L plastikflaske med låg. Der tilsættes 
500 mL destilleret vand. Prøven rystes og henstilles til bundfældning. 
 
Et filterpapir vejes og sættes i en tragt over et måleglas. 
 
Væsken hældes over i filterpapiret. Der tilsættes igen 500 mL destilleret vand til asken og 
prøven omrystes og henstilles. 
 
Væsken hældes over i det samme filter og der tilsættes igen 500 destilleret vand til asken 
og prøven omrystes. 
 
Hele prøven hældes over i filteret og stilles til afdrypning.  
 
Når asken er næste tør stilles filteret med asken i varmeskab ved 105°C natten over. 
 
Asken og filteret vejes og vægten noteres.

Appendix 2E – Water content 
 
 
 
Vandindhold i jord 
 
 
A 
Princip 
 
I forbindelse med den elektrodialytisk jordrensning har jordens vandindhold stor 
betydning, da den elektriske strøm ledes gennem jorden via jordvæsken. Er 
vandindholdet i jorden mindre end ca. 15 %, er det nødvendigt for forsøgets 
gennemførelse at tilføre jorden ekstra væske.   
 
B 
Specielt apparatur 
 
Til analysen benyttes et varmeskab. 
 
C 
Analysens udførelse 
 
Ved meget præcis måling: 
 
3 bægerglas stilles i varmeskab ved 105C natten over. 
Bægerglassene stilles til afkøling i eksikator og vejes på analysevægt.  
 
Dette gøres vha. en tang, fingrene afsætter fugt.   
 
Der afvejes 5 g jord i hvert bægerglas og prøverne tørres ved 105C i varmeskab. 
Bægerglassene med den tørrede jord vejes på analyse vægt efter afkøling i 
eksikator. 
 
Ved mindre præcis måling (mest anvendt): 
 
3 bægerglas afvejes på teknisk vægt. Der overføres en vis mængde til hvert 
bægerglas, som igen vejes på teknisk vægt. Bægerglassene stilles i varmeskab 
natten over ved 105C. Bægerglassene med den tørrede jord vejes efter afkøling 
på teknisk vægt. 
 
D 
Beregning af resultat 
 
Vandindholdet angives i procent af vægten af den fugtige jord.

Appendix 2G – Solubilisation  
 
 
13 
 
Oplukning af jordprøve efter DS 259 
 
 
A 
Princip 
 
Denne oplukningsmetode er en Dansk Standard (DS 259) til bestemmelse af 
syreopløselige metaller i jorden. Metalkoncentrationen fundet ved denne metode 
svarer til en “total-koncentration” af det pågældende metal i jorden. Ved en 
fuldstændig destruktion af jorden, vil man dog have mulighed for at finde en 
højere værdi af de forskellige metaller, så DS 259 giver derfor snarere et fastlagt 
niveau for en metalkoncentration, end en fuldstændig bestemmelse af metal-
koncentrationen i jorden. 
 
 
 
B 
Specielt apparatur 
 
Til målingen benyttes et atomabsorptionsspektrofotometer (AAS). 
 
 
 
C 
Kemikalie sikkerhed 
 
Salpetersyre - Brandnærende; Ætsende; Brandfarlig ved kontakt med 
brandbare stoffer. Alvorlig ætsningsfare. Undgå indånding af dampe. Brug 
syrehandsker, plastikforklæder, sikkerhedsbriller og stinkskab ved afmåling. 
 
Salpetersyre halv konc. (1:1) - Lokalirriterende; Irriterer øjnene og huden. 
Brug engangshandsker, sikkerhedsbriller og stinkskab ved afmåling. 
 
Læs kemikaliebrugsanvisningen før arbejdet begynder. 
 
 
 
D 
Reagenser 
 
1) 
Salpetersyre halvkonc, (1:1) HNO3: 
500 mL koncentreret HNO3 overføres med måleglas til en 1000,00 mL målekolbe 
som er ½ fyldt med destilleret vand. Der blandes godt og tilsættes vand til 
mærket. Efter blanding overføres opløsningen til en plastikflaske og mærkes. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
E 
Analysens udførelse

(skal foregå i stinkskab).

Cement and Concrete 
SEU-SBT-RILEM Doctoral Course, Subote R&D Centre (SBT), Nanjing, 25 Aug - 1 Sept 2011 
Organized by Ole Mejlhede Jensen, Wei Sun and Changwen Miao 
 
 
Module 16 
Sunday 28 August, 1545 – 1730 
Module 20 
Monday 29 August, 1545 – 1730 
 
 
Laboratory exercises 
Marianne Tange Hasholt, Technical University of Denmark 
Tian Qian, Subote R&D Centre 
 
Objective 
The objective is twofold: 
• To make the student familiar with two specific test methods (see below) 
• To give the student practical experience with laboratory testing related to concrete 
durability. 
The practical experience can be used as starting point for discussion about general aspects of 
laboratory testing, also in other course modules. This can be aspects such as laboratory testing 
vs. field exposure, pitfalls in laboratory work, uncertainty of measurements, how information 
from different tests can be combined, etc. 
 
Students will work in individual groups (approx. 7 persons). The groups will present their 
work in plenum Thursday 1 (module 31). 
 
Exercises 
Each group is given a set of paste and concrete samples (one binder combination per group), 
which they are asked to characterize as regards 
• degree of hydration (no standard method, see enclosed course note) 
• resistance to chloride ingress (NT BUILD 492) 
• strength 
Afterwards groups will share results, so they can compare properties for different mix designs. 
 
Preparations 
For the laboratory work, you need to bring suitable laboratory clothing with you. We will 
provide safety goggles and gloves. 
 
The following material should be read before the lecture (homework): 
 
• Course note: How to measure degree of hydration 
• NT BUILD 492: Concrete, mortar and cement-based repair materials: Chloride migration 
coefficient from non-steady-state migration experiments (1999). 
 
 
 
16.08.2011  
Appendix 2H - Degree of hydration
==PAGE:110==
Cement and Concrete 
SEU-SBT-RILEM Doctoral Course, Subote R&D Centre (SBT), Nanjing, 25 Aug - 1 Sept 2011 
Organized by Ole Mejlhede Jensen, Wei Sun and Changwen Miao 
 
 
Note: 
How to measure degree of hydration 
 
 
Instruction – laboratory work 
 
Scope 
The test method measures the degree of hydration of cement in a cement paste sample beyond 
a few days of hardening. The degree of hydration is calculated from measurements of the 
amount of physically and chemically bound water relative to cement.  
 
Degree of hydration is defined according to Powers’ model, which is described in module 3. 
 
Paste samples 
Preparation of paste samples is not part of the exercise, but they are made the following way: 
Cement, water, and possibly fly ash or silica fume are weighed in proportions similar to the 
corresponding concrete mix used for chloride migration testing, but the amounts are adjusted 
to yield approximately 0.4 l of paste. Powder(s) and water are mixed for 4 minutes in a special 
paste mixer. The paste is poured into moulds and sealed. The moulds are rotated till set to 
maintain homogeneity of the cement paste (in particular to avoid bleeding). Afterwards the 
samples are stored together with the concrete specimens as to expose them to identical 
temperature conditions until testing, so they develop the same maturity. 
 
Equipment 
• 
Scale, 0.0001 g accuracy 
• 
Vacuum pump 
• 
Desiccator  
• 
Oven, 105°C 
• 
Furnace 1050°C 
• 
Weighing bottle 
• 
Crucible 
• 
Hammer and steel plate 
 
 
 
16.08.2011 
==PAGE:111==
Cement and Concrete 
SEU-SBT-RILEM Doctoral Course, Subote R&D Centre (SBT), Nanjing, 25 Aug - 1 Sept 2011 
Organized by Ole Mejlhede Jensen, Wei Sun and Changwen Miao 
 
 
 
Day 1 
1. Determine the mass of the weighing bottle (mw.b.). 
2. Crush the paste sample with a hammer*. 
3. Put the crushed sample in the weighing bottle and determine the mass (mw.b. + m1). 
4. Place the sample in the weighing bottle in a desiccator and evacuate it**. 
5. Place the weighing bottle with the cement paste sample in a 105°C oven. 
 
Day 2 
1. After approximately 24 h in the oven, take the weighing bottle out, and leave the 
weighing bottle and sample to cool to near room temperature. 
2. Determine the mass of the weighing bottle with the sample (mw.b. + m2). 
3. Determine the mass of the crucible (mcru). 
4. Put the sample in the crucible. 
5. Determine the mass of the crucible with the sample (mcru + m3) 
6. Place the crucible with the sample in a 1050°C furnace for 1 hour***. 
7. Take the crucible with the sample out of the furnace***, leave it on the special heat 
resistant surface for 5 minutes. 
8. Place the crucible with the sample in a desiccator and allow it to cool to approx. room 
temperature. 
9. Determine the mass of the crucible with the sample (mcru + m4). 
 
Note 
* 
In general, through all experiments, try to expose the sample as little as possible to the 
atmosphere, i.e. minimize the time when it is being prepared and cover it when possible. 
This should minimize evaporation and carbonation, as both phenomena will influence the 
results. 
 
** Vacuum will quickly lower the relative humidity in the sample and thereby stop 
hydration. In this experiment, we use paste samples, which have a maturity of 2 weeks or 
more. Therefore it is not a problem that it takes up to 1 hour to stop the hydration. For 
young paste samples, e.g. 1-2 days after mixing, other methods like e.g. freezing the 
sample in liquid nitrogen and subsequently freeze drying it may be considered. A 
common procedure in the literature is to put the paste sample directly into the 105°C 
oven, but this is not recommended: during elevation of temperature the hydration is 
accelerated and may thus lead to systematic errors. 
 
*** 1050°C is a very high temperature! Even special oven gloves may deteriorate if you bring 
them too close to the open furnace or if you touch the crucible right after it has left the 
furnace. Therefore, be very careful with the handling.

Instruction – calculations 
 
Scope 
The objective is to determine: 
 
• amount of physically bound water 
mphys. Using the same terms as in 
Powers’ diagram, this is the sum of 
capillary water and gel water. 
• amount of chemically bound water 
mchem. Using the same terms as in 
Powers’ diagram, this is the amount 
of water chemically bound in the gel 
solid. 
 
From the amount of chemically bound 
water, it is possible to deduce the degree 
of hydration, α. 
 
 
 
Figure A: Powers’ diagram. 
 
In the following description of calculations, it is assumed that cement is the only powder in 
the paste. If the powder combination is not known, this can be used as a starting point. 
 
 
 
16.08.2011 
==PAGE:115==
Cement and Concrete 
SEU-SBT-RILEM Doctoral Course, Subote R&D Centre (SBT), Nanjing, 25 Aug - 1 Sept 2011 
Organized by Ole Mejlhede Jensen, Wei Sun and Changwen Miao 
 
 
 
1. Loss on ignition (LOI) 
The test method is based on the assumption that all mass changes are due to release of water, 
i.e. 
 
• the mass change from the test start to the sample is removed from the 105°C oven is due to 
release of physically bound water 
• the mass change during the exposure in the 1050°C oven is due to the release of 
chemically bound water. 
 
At the same time, it is assumed that the cement mass is unchanged during the test. However, 
this is in general not true. If dry cement is exposed to 1050°C, there will be a loss on ignition 
(LOI), normally in the range 1-3 %. This can be due to the following reasons: 
 
• The cement contains limestone filler, which at high temperatures decomposes to CaO and 
CO2, where CO2 is released from the sample 
• The cement contains gypsum, which binds water, and it dehydrates at high temperatures 
CaSO4·2H2O (s)  → CaSO4·½H2O (s) + 1½H2O (g) and 
CaSO4·½H2O (s)  → CaSO4 (s) + ½H2O (g) 
 
• If the cement has been exposed to moisture before mixing, e.g. moisture in the air, it may 
be slightly pre-hydrated 
 
For other powders, there may also be LOI, in fly ash this is e.g. due to small amounts of 
unburned coal. 
 
If LOI of the cement is known, this should be taken into account. LOI is normally stated 
relative to mass before ignition [%]. 
 
 
 
16.08.2011 
==PAGE:116==
Cement and Concrete 
SEU-SBT-RILEM Doctoral Course, Subote R&D Centre (SBT), Nanjing, 25 Aug - 1 Sept 2011 
Organized by Ole Mejlhede Jensen, Wei Sun and Changwen Miao 
 
 
 
2. Chemically bound water 
The amount of chemically bound water (g per g cement) is calculated as described below. 
Terminology is defined in figure B. 
Figure B: Definition of terms in calculations of chemically bound water. 
 
a. Determine m3 and m4: 
mi = (mcru + mi) – mcru 
b. Mass change during ignition: 
m3-4 = m3 – m4 
c. The mass of the sample left in the crucible after ignition is in principle equal to the mass 
of the cement used to produce it, mcement 1. However, it has to be corrected for LOI. 
mcement 1 = m4/(1-LOI) 
d. The absolute amount of chemically bound water: 
mchem, abs = m3-4 – m cement 1·LOI 
e. The relative amount of chemically bound water: 
mchem = mchem,abs/m cement 1 
 
 
 
16.08.2011 
==PAGE:117==
Cement and Concrete 
SEU-SBT-RILEM Doctoral Course, Subote R&D Centre (SBT), Nanjing, 25 Aug - 1 Sept 2011 
Organized by Ole Mejlhede Jensen, Wei Sun and Changwen Miao 
 
 
 
3. Physically bound water 
The amount of physically bound water (g per g cement) is calculated as follows (see also 
figure C for definition of terminology): 
Figure C: Definition of terms in calculations of physically bound water. 
 
a. Determine m1 and m2: 
mi = (mw.b. + mi) – mw.b. 
b. Mass change from test start to removal from 105°C oven: m1-2 = m1 – m2 
c. After exposure at 105°C, the remaining sample consists of unhydrated cement and gel 
solid, that means the mass equals the sum of the original cement mcement 2 and the 
chemically bound water. 
mcement 2 = m2/(1+mchem) 
d. The absolute amount of physically bound water, mphys, abs: 
mphys, abs = m1-2 
e. The relative amount of physically bound water: 
mphys = mphys, abs/mcement 2 
 
 
 
16.08.2011 
==PAGE:118==
Cement and Concrete 
SEU-SBT-RILEM Doctoral Course, Subote R&D Centre (SBT), Nanjing, 25 Aug - 1 Sept 2011 
Organized by Ole Mejlhede Jensen, Wei Sun and Changwen Miao 
 
 
 
4. Results 
From the above mentioned calculations, the following is known: 
 
• 
physically bound water mphys (g per g powder) 
• 
chemically bound water mchem (g per g powder) 
 
Based on this, it is possible to calculate: 
 
• 
initial w/c (or w/p): 
sum of physically and chemically bound water (g per g powder): 
w/c = mphys + mchem 
 
• 
degree of hydration, α: 
amount of chemically bound water relative to amount of chemically bound water at full 
hydration ~ 0.23 g per g cement 
α = mchem/0.23 (only correct for paste with cement as only powder) 
 
It is now possible to sketch Powers’ diagram for the relevant binder combination and indicate 
degree of hydration at time of testing (vertical line). Note, Powers’ diagram is based on 
volume, not mass. Use the densities in Table A for conversion.

NORDTEST METHOD 
NT BUILD 492 
1 
NT BUILD 492 
 
 
Approved 1999–11 
 
 
 
 
1(8) 
 
CONCRETE, MORTAR AND CEMENT-BASED REPAIR 
MATERIALS: 
CHLORIDE MIGRATION COEFFICIENT FROM 
NON-STEADY-STATE MIGRATION EXPERIMENTS 
 
 
Key words: Chlorides, concrete, diffusion, mortar, repair materials, migration, test method 
 
UDC 691.32/691.53/691.54 
 
 
 
1 
SCOPE 
 
This procedure is for determination of the chloride migration 
coefficient in concrete, mortar or cement-based repair mate- 
rials from non-steady-state migration experiments. 
 
 
2 
FIELD OF APPLICATION 
 
The method is applicable to hardened specimens cast in the 
laboratory or drilled from field structures. The chloride migra- 
tion coefficient determined by the method is a measure of the 
resistance of the tested material to chloride penetration. This 
non-steady-state migration coefficient cannot be directly com- 
pared with chloride diffusion coefficients obtained from the 
other test methods, such as the non-steady-state immersion 
test or the steady-state migration test. 
 
 
3 
REFERENCES 
 
/1/ NT BUILD 201, ”Concrete: Making and curing of moulded 
test specimens for strength tests”, 2nd ed., Approved 
1984-05. 
/2/ NT BUILD 202, ”Concrete, hardened: Sampling and treat- 
ment of cores for strength tests”, 2nd ed., Approved 1984- 
05. 
/3/ NT BUILD 208, ”Concrete, hardened: Chloride content”, 
2nd ed., Approved 1984-05. 
/4/ Tang, L and Sørensen, H.E., ”Evaluation of the Rapid Test 
Methods for Chloride Diffusion Coefficient of Concrete, 
NORDTEST Project No. 1388-98”, SP Report 1998:42, 
SP  Swedish  National  Testing  and  Research  Institute, 
Borås, Sweden, 1998. 
 
 
4 
DEFINITIONS 
 
Migration: The movement of ions under the action of an 
external electrical field. 
Diffusion: The movement of molecules or ions under a con- 
centration gradient or, more strictly, chemical potential, from a 
high concentration zone to a low concentration zone. 
5 
SAMPLING 
 
The method requires cylindrical specimens with a diameter of 
100 mm and a thickness of 50 mm, sliced from cast cylinders 
or drilled cores with a minimum length of 100 mm. The 
cylinders and cores should meet the requirements described 
in NT BUILD 201 and NT BUILD 202 respectively. Three 
specimens should be used in the test. 
 
 
6 
TEST METHOD 
 
6.1 
Principle 
 
An external electrical potential is applied axially across the 
specimen and forces the chloride ions outside to migrate into 
the specimen. After a certain test duration, the specimen is 
axially split and a silver nitrate solution is sprayed on to one 
of the freshly split sections. The chloride penetration depth 
can then be measured from the visible white silver chloride 
precipitation, after which the chloride migration coefficient can 
be calculated from this penetration depth. 
 
 
6.2 
Reagents and apparatus 
 
6.2.1 Reagents 
 
• 
Distilled or de-ionised water. 
• 
Calcium hydroxide: Ca(OH)2, technical quality. 
• 
Sodium chloride: NaCl, chemical quality. 
• 
Sodium hydroxide: NaOH, chemical quality. 
• 
Silver nitrate: AgNO3, chemical quality. 
• 
Chemicals for chloride analysis as required by the test 
method employed (optional, see 6.4.6). 
 
 
6.2.2 Apparatus 
 
• 
Water-cooled diamond saw. 
 
• 
Vacuum container: capable of containing at least three 
specimens. 
• 
Vacuum pump: capable of maintaining a pressure of less 
than 50 mbar (5 kPa) in the container. 
 
 
 
Published by NORDTEST 
P.O. Box 116  FIN–02151 ESPOO FINLAND 
Phone + 358 9 455 4600 
Fax + 358 9 455 4272 
ISSN 0283–7153 
Proj. 1388–98 
==PAGE:120==
NORDTEST METHOD 
NT BUILD 492 
2 
 
 
• 
Migration set-up: One design (see Appendix 1) includes 
the following parts: 
– Silicone  rubber  sleeve:  inner/outer  diameter  100/ 
115 mm, about 150 mm long. 
– Clamp: diameter range 105 ∼ 115, 20 mm wide, stain- 
less steel (see Figure 2 in Appendix 1). 
– Catholyte reservoir: plastic box, 370 × 270 × 280 mm 
(length × width × height). 
– Plastic support: (see Figure 3 in Appendix 1). 
 
– Cathode: stainless steel plate (see Figure 3 in Appen- 
dix 1), about 0.5 mm thick. 
– Anode: stainless steel mesh or plate with holes (see 
Figure 4 in Appendix 1), about 0.5 mm thick. 
Other designs are acceptable, provided that temperatures 
of the specimen and solutions during the test can be 
maintained in the range of 20 to 25 °C (see 6.4.2). 
• 
Power supply: capable of supplying 0 ∼ 60 V DC regulated 
voltage with an accuracy of ±0.1 V. 
• 
Ammeter: capable of displaying current to ±1 mA. 
 
• 
Thermometer or thermocouple with readout device capa- 
ble of reading to ±1 °C. 
• 
Any suitable device for splitting the specimen. 
 
• 
Spray bottle. 
• 
Slide calliper with a precision of ±0.1 mm. 
 
• 
Ruler with a minimum scale of 1 mm. 
 
• 
Equipment for chloride analysis as required by the test 
method employed (optional, see 6.4.6). 
 
 
 
6.3 
Preparation of the test specimen 
 
6.3.1 Test specimen 
 
If a drilled core  is  used,  the  outermost  approximately 
10 ∼ 20 mm thick layer should be cut off (Note 1) and the next 
50 ± 2 mm thick slice should be cut as the test specimen. The 
end surface that was nearer to the outermost layer is the one 
to be exposed to the chloride solution (catholyte). 
If a ∅100 × 100 mm cast cylinder is used, cut a 50 ± 2 mm 
thick slice from the central portion of the cylinder as the test 
specimen. The end surface that was nearer to the as-cast 
surface is the one to be exposed to the chloride solution 
(catholyte). 
If a ∅100 × 200 mm cast cylinder is used, prepare the test 
specimen by first cutting the cylinder into two halves (i.e. into 
two ∅100 × 100 mm cylinders), and then cutting a 50 ± 2 mm 
thick slice from one half. The end surface that was nearer to 
the first cut (the middle surface) is the one to be exposed to 
the chloride solution (catholyte). 
Measure  the  thickness  with  a  slide  calliper  and  read  to 
0.1 mm. 
 
Note 1: The term ‘cut’ here means to saw perpendicularly to 
the axis of a core or cylinder, using a water-cooled diamond 
saw. 
6.3.2   Preconditioning 
 
After sawing, brush and wash away any burrs from the sur- 
faces of the specimen, and wipe off excess water from the 
surfaces of the specimen. When the specimens are surface-
dry, place them in the vacuum container for vacuum 
treatment. Both end surfaces must be exposed. Reduce the 
absolute pressure in the vacuum container to a pressure in 
the range of 10–50 mbar (1–5 kPa) within a few minutes. 
Maintain the vacuum for three hours and then, with the vac- 
uum pump still running, fill the container with the saturated 
Ca(OH)2  solution (by dissolving an excess of calcium hy- 
droxide in distilled or de-ionised water) so as to immerse all 
the specimens. Maintain the vacuum for a further hour before 
allowing air to re-enter the container. Keep the specimens in 
the solution for 18 ± 2 hours. 
 
 
6.4 
Procedure 
 
6.4.1 Catholyte and anolyte 
 
The catholyte solution is 10 % NaCl by mass in tap water 
(100 g NaCl in 900 g water, about 2 N) and the anolyte solu- 
tion is 0.3 N NaOH in distilled or de-ionised water (approxi- 
mately 12 g NaOH in 1 litre water). Store the solutions at a 
temperature of 20–25 °C. 
 
 
6.4.2 Temperature 
 
Maintain the temperatures of the specimen and solutions in 
the range of 20–25 °C during the test. 
 
 
6.4.3 Preparation of the test 
 
• 
Fill the catholyte reservoir with about 12 litres of 10 % 
NaCl solution. 
• 
Fit the rubber sleeve on the specimen as shown in Fig- 
ure 4 in Appendix 1 and secure it with two clamps. If the 
curved surface of the specimen is not smooth, or there 
are defects on the curved surface which could result in 
significant leakage, apply a line of silicone sealant to im- 
prove the tightness. 
• 
Place the specimen on the plastic support in the catholyte 
reservoir (see Figure 1 in Appendix 1). 
• 
Fill the sleeve above the specimen with 300 ml anolyte 
solution (0.3 M NaOH). 
• 
Immerse the anode in the anolyte solution. 
 
• 
Connect the cathode to the negative pole and the anode 
to the positive pole of the power supply. 
 
 
6.4.4 Migration test 
 
• 
Turn on the power, with the voltage preset at 30 V, and 
record the initial current through each specimen. 
• 
Adjust the voltage if necessary (as shown in Table 1 in 
Appendix 2). After adjustment, note the value of the initial 
current again.

shown by the thermometer or thermocouple.

current (see Table 1 in Appendix 2).

6.6 
Accuracy 
 
6.6.1 Repeatability 
 
The coefficient of variation of repeatability is 9 %, according to 
the results from the Nordic round-robin test between six 
laboratories /4/. 
 
 
 
6.6.2 Reproducibility 
 
The coefficient of variation of reproducibility is 13 % for 
Portland cement concrete or for concrete mixed with silica 
fume, and 24 % for concrete mixed with slag cement, accord- 
ing to the results from the Nordic round-robin test between six 
laboratories /4/. 
 
 
 
6.7 
Test report 
 
The test report should, if known, include the following informa- 
tion: 
a) Name and address of the test laboratory. 
 
b) Date and identification number of the test report. 
c) Name and address of the organisation or person who 
ordered the test. 
d) Name and address of the manufacturer or supplier of the 
material or object tested. 
e) Date of arrival of the material or object tested. 
 
f) Description of the material or object tested, including sam- 
pling, composition, and curing age. 
g) Purpose of the test. 
 
h) Test method. 
 
i) Any deviation from the test method. 
 
j) Name and address of the person who performed the test. 
 
k) Date of the test. 
 
l) Test results, including the specimen dimensions, applied 
voltage, initial and final currents, initial and final tempera- 
tures, average and individual data of penetration depth and 
migration coefficient. 
m) Any observation of an abnormal penetration front due to a 
defect in the specimen. 
n) Optional information about surface chloride content. 
 
o) Date and signature.

4. Eksperimenter
4.1   Udtagning og neddeling af repræ-
sentativ prøve af sand 
4.1.1 Apparatur og materialer 
- 
Sanddeler 
- 
Tør sandprøve (til to grupper) 
 
4.1.2   Fremgangsmåde 
I udsugningskabinen udtages en repræsentativ 
sandprøve på ca. 500 g fra spanden med tørt 
sand ved at fylde målebægeret op til randen. 
Prøven neddeles til to prøver á ca. 250 g ved 
hjælp af sanddeler. Prøven hældes over i grup-
pens plastbeholder og anvendes til sigteanaly-
sen. Plastbeholdere står i udsugningskabinen. 
4.2 Bestemmelse af kornstørrelsesfor-
deling ved sigteanalyse 
4.2.1   Apparatur og materialer 
- 
Sigtesøjle 
- 
Rystemaskine 
- 
Vægt 
- 
Tør sandprøve, ca. 250 g 
 
4.2.2 Fremgangsmåde 
Den neddelte sandprøve på ca. 250 g vejes. Det 
undersøges omhyggeligt om alle sigter er rene 
og tørre. Hver sigte vejes og sigterne sættes 
sammen til en sigtesøjle hvor sigten med den 
største maskevidde placeres øverst. Sandprø-
ven hældes i toppen af sigtesøjlen, låget sættes 
på og der rystes i ca. 2 min i rystemaskinen. 
Hver sigte med tilbageholdt sand vejes, og 
sigteresten findes ved subtraktion af den tom-
me sigtes vægt. Resultatet indføres i et sigtedi-
agram hvor punkterne forbindes med rette 
linjer. Resultater anføres i skema G1 i bilag 1. 
Kornkurven optegnes på tavlen med forskelligt 
farvet kridt for hver gruppe, og det undersøges 
om kurven ligger indenfor grænsekurverne. 
4.3   Fastlæggelse af vandmættet, over-
fladetør (s.s.d.) tilstand  
4.3.1 Apparatur og materialer 
- 
Kogeplade 
- 
Stegepande 
- 
Tragt 
- 
Metalbakke 
- 
Ske til omrøring 
- 
Kegleform med stamper 
- 
Vandlagret sand, ca. 1 kg 
 
4.3.2 Fremgangsmåde 
Der udtages en prøve på ca. 1 kg sand fra 
spanden med vandlagret sand ved at fylde 
målebægeret op til randen. Sandprøven hældes 
derefter over i gruppens egen plastbeholder, 
der står ved spanden med vandlagret sand. 
Sandet spredes ud på stegepanden og udtørres 
forsigtigt ved svag varme under omrøring for 
at sikre en jævn udtørring. Tørringen afbrydes 
før det tidspunkt hvor sandkornene tilsynela-
dende ikke længere klæber til hinanden. Sandet 
skal afkøles i metalbakken under omrøring før 
det ifyldes den kegleform, der er vist i fig. 6. 
Efter at sandet er løst ifyldt formen stryges 
langs overkanten og der stampes let 25 gange 
med stamperen. Der efterfyldes ikke. Formen 
løftes af, og såfremt sandkeglen ikke falder 
sammen, er der stadig overfladevand tilstede 
hvorfor udtørringen skal fortsættes som oven-
for beskrevet indtil sandkeglen netop falder 
sammen når formen fjernes, jf. fig. 7. Sandet 
siges nu at være i vandmættet, overfladetør 
(s.s.d.) tilstand. Metalbakken med sand i s.s.d.-
tilstand dækkes med bakkens låg eller plastfo-
lie for at undgå yderligere udtørring indtil de 
øvrige forsøg er udført. 
 
 
Fig. 6. Kegleform og stamper. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:133==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 7. Sandkegle i fire forskellige tilstande: 
(A) meget fugtig tilstand hvor sandkeglen næ-
sten har bevaret metalkeglens form, (B) fugtig 
tilstand hvor et markant sammenfald ses, (C) 
vandmættet, overfladetør tilstand hvor der er 
et næsten komplet sammenfald men hvor en 
spids top er synlig, (D) næsten ovntør tilstand 
med komplet sammenfald og uden spids top. 
4.4   Bestemmelse af vandabsorptions-
evne 
4.4.1 Apparatur og materialer 
- 
Keramikskål 
- 
Ske til omrøring 
- 
Stegepande 
- 
Kogeplade 
- 
Vægt 
- 
Sand i s.s.d.-tilstand 
 
4.4.2   Fremgangsmåde 
Til dette forsøg benyttes ca. 250 g sand i 
vandmættet, overfladetør tilstand fra forrige 
forsøg. Mængden afvejes og prøven udtørres 
til konstant masse i et varmeskab ved 105  5 
C. Prøvens tørmasse noteres. Af tidsmæssige 
grunde fremskyndes udtørringen af sandet ved 
brug af stegepanden. Denne metode er mindre 
nøjagtig end udtørring i varmeskab og bør ikke 
bruges i praksis. I skema G2 i bilag 2 anføres 
forsøgsresultater. Disse skrives ligeledes på 
tavlen i forsøgslokalet. 
4.5   Bestemmelse densitet i s.s.d.-
tilstand 
4.5.1 Apparatur og materialer 
- 
Metalbakke 
- 
Konisk kolbe 
- 
Målenedstik  
- 
Glasspatel til omrøring 
- 
Vægt 
- 
Sprøjteflaske 
- 
Sand i s.s.d.-tilstand 
4.5.2 Fremgangsmåde 
Kolben stilles på et vandret underlag og fyldes 
med vand til målenedstikkets spids. Massen af 
kolbe + målenedstik + vand (m0) bestemmes. 
Ca. 2/3 af vandet hældes fra og en afvejet 
mængde sand på ca. 600 g i vandmættet, over-
fladetør tilstand hældes i kolben under samti-
dig omrøring. Kolben rystes og rulles indtil al 
luft er drevet ud af materialet. Der fyldes efter 
med vand til målenedstikkets  spids. Massen af 
kolbe + målenedstik + sand + vand (m1) be-
stemmes. Resultater anføres i skema G3 i bilag 
2. 
4.6   Bestemmelse af vandindhold 
4.6.1 Apparatur og materialer 
- 
Keramikskål 
- 
Ske til omrøring 
- 
Stegepande 
- 
Kogeplade 
- 
Vægt 
- 
Fugtigt sand, ca. 0,5 kg 
4.6.2 Fremgangsmåde 
Der udtages en prøve på ca. 0,5 kg sand fra 
spanden med fugtigt sand ved at fylde måle-
bægeret op til randen. Sandprøven hældes 
derefter over i gruppens egen plastbeholder, 
der står ved spanden med fugtigt sand. Ca. 200 
g fugtigt sand afvejes hvorefter prøven udtør-
res til konstant vægt i et varmeskab ved 105  
5 C. Prøvens tørmasse noteres. Af tidsmæssi-
ge grunde fremskyndes udtørringen af sandet 
ved brug af stegepande. Denne metode er min-
dre nøjagtig end udtørring i varmeskab og bør 
ikke bruges i praksis. Resultater anføres i ske-
ma G4 i bilag 2 og på tavlen i forsøgslokalet. 
 
Når målearbejdet er færdigt, indtastes må-
leresultater i et resultatskema der ligger 
under fildeling på Campusnet. Husk at 
gemme det indtastede regneark på egen PC 
og ’uploade’ det indtastede regneark på 
CampusNet.

==PAGE:145==
 
 
Appendix 3I – Grading curves 
 
Grading curve 
A particle size distribution test has been carried out on the untreated and milled sewage sludge ash, 
silica fume, coal fly ash and Aalborg Portland basis cement in order to control how the milling 
effects the size distribution of the ash and see the differences if any . The size distribution is made 
with a lazerdifractometer. The untreated ash (Lynetten-NY-3 and Lynetten-NY-4) is tested in 
October 2014 and the milled ash, silica fume, coal fly ash and Basis cement is tested in January 
2015. The results for Lynetten-NY-3 and Lynetten-NY-4 can be seen in the result section and 
shows that the two ashes are very similar. When looking at the milled ash, silica fume, coal fly ash 
and Basis cement is a flat plateau seen at about 80-90 % of their volumes. According to (annemette) 
should the grading curve not be like this for a ash milled for 30 seconds. In order to control the 
apparatus was the tests made again after the equipment had been cleaned. The result for the new 
grading curves looked like the first ones and the results is therefore not a part of this report.
